5.11.1 Test description and applicability 5 The purpose of the test is to evaluate the user peak downlink throughput as a function of the fronthaul transport latency 6 (i.e. one-way transmission delay between O-RU and O-DU at OpenFH (CUSM-plane interface), and to identify the 7 maximum applicable fronthaul transport latency.  Since the fronthaul transport latency corresponds with the distance 8 (fiber length) between O-RU and O-DU, the results of test can be also used to identify the maximum distance (fiber 9 length) with an acceptable degradation of user peak downlink throughput. 10 5.11.2 Test setup and configuration 11 The network setup is single cell scenario (i.e. isolated cell without any inter-cell interference – see Section 3.7) with 12 stationary UE (real or emulated UE) placed in the excellent radio condition as defined in Section 3.6 - SINR should be 13 considered in case of downlink. Within the cell there should be only one active UE downloading data from the application 14 server. The application server should be placed as close as possible to the core/core emulator and connected to the 15 core/core emulator via transport link with sufficient capacity not limiting the expected data throughput. The test is suitable 16 for lab as well as field environment. 17 The reference measurement points of the fronthaul latency (R1/R4 – Transmit/Receive interface at O-DU (CU-plane); 18 R2/R3 – Receive/Transmit interfaces at O-RU (CU-plane)) [16] are shown in Figure 5-4. Transmission delay between 19 O-RU and O-DU are specified as T12 (downlink direction) and T34 (uplink direction). Transmission delay should be 20 symmetrical and equal in both directions. The transmission delay encompasses only the time from when a bit leaves the 21 sender (R1/ R3) until it is received at the receiver (R2/ R4). 22  23 
 24 Figure 5-4 Definition of reference measurement points for fronthaul latency [16] 25  26 Test configuration: The test configuration is not specified. The utilized test configuration (parameters) should be 27 recorded in the test report.  28 Laboratory setup: The radio conditions of UE can be modified by a variable attenuator/fading generator inserted between 29 the antenna connectors of O-RU and UE. The minimum attenuation of radio signal should be set to achieve the excellent 30 radio conditions (SINR as defined in Section 3.6), but the minimum coupling loss (see Section 3.6) should not be 31 exceeded. The UE should be placed inside RF shielded box or RF shielded room if the UE is not connected via cable.  32

The minimum fronthaul latency (one-way transmission delay between O-DU and O-RU) should be set using a 18 network impairment emulator or a fibre with corresponding length (assuming typical delay of fibre around 5 us per 19 kilometre).  20 5. The UEs should be turned off or set to airplane mode, if possible, to empty the buffers. The uplink full-buffer UDP 21 data transmission from UE to the application server is started. The application server should receive data from UE.  22 6. All the required performance data (incl. the signalling and control data) as specified in the following “Test 23 requirements” section is measured and captured at UE, eNB/gNB and Application server using logging/measurement 24 tools.  25 7. The capture of log data is stopped. The uplink full-buffer UDP data transmission from application server to UE is 26 stopped.  27 8. The fronthaul latency is increased by 20 us if no degradation of user peak uplink throughput was observer in the 28 previous measurement. As soon as a degradation of user peak uplink throughout will be observed, the fronthaul 29 latency is increased only by 5 us in order to capture fine-grained log data.  30 9.  Steps 5 to 8 are repeated until the total degradation of user peak uplink throughput is less than 30%. The KPIs 31 measured with the minim fronthaul latency are used as a baseline (100%) for calculation of the degradation. 32 10. [Optional] Steps 4 to 9 are repeated for uplink full-buffer TCP data transmission.   33 5.12.4 Test requirements (expected results) 34 In addition to the common minimum set of configuration parameters (see Section 3.3), the following metrics and counters 35 should be recorded and reported for the performance assessment. 36  37 UE side (real or emulated UE): 38 • Radio parameters such as RSRP, RSRQ, CQI, PDSCH SINR (average sample per second) 39

5.13 Impact of midhaul latency on downlink peak throughout  1 
5.13.1 Test description and applicability 2 The purpose of the test is to evaluate the user peak downlink throughput as a function of the midhaul transport latency 3 (i.e. one-way transmission delay between O-DU and O-CU at F1 interface). Since the midhaul transport latency 4 corresponds with the distance (fiber length) between O-DU and O-CU, the results of test can be also used to identify the 5 maximum distance (fiber length) with an acceptable degradation of user peak downlink throughput. Note that the midhaul 6 transport exists only if O-DU and O-CU are not a combined entity, and the interface F1 between O-DU and O-CU is 7 exposed. The test does not support LTE network architecture because an interface providing means for interconnecting 8 LTE O-DU/DU and LTE CU has not been specified yet neither in 3GPP nor in O-RAN ALLIANCE. Note that W1 9 interface [33] has been specified only for 5G NSA Options 4 and 7 network architectures. The test is suitable only for 5G 10 NSA (Option 3/3a/3x) and 5G SA network architectures as defined in Chapter 3. 11 5.13.2 Test setup and configuration 12 The network setup is single cell scenario (i.e. isolated cell without any inter-cell interference – see Section 3.7) with 13 stationary UE (real or emulated UE) placed in the excellent radio condition as defined in Section 3.6 - SINR should be 14 considered in case of downlink. Within the cell there should be only one active UE downloading data from the application 15 server. The application server should be placed as close as possible to the core/core emulator and connected to the 16 core/core emulator via transport link with sufficient capacity not limiting the expected data throughput. The test is suitable 17 for lab as well as field environment. 18 The midhaul latency encompasses only the time from when a bit leaves O-CU until it is received at O-DU. 19  20 Test configuration: The test configuration is not specified. The utilized test configuration (parameters) should be 21 recorded in the test report.  22 Laboratory setup: The radio conditions of UE can be modified by a variable attenuator/fading generator inserted between 23 the antenna connectors of O-RU and UE. The minimum attenuation of radio signal should be set to achieve the excellent 24 radio conditions (SINR as defined in Section 3.6), but the minimum coupling loss (see Section 3.6) should not be 25 exceeded. The UE should be placed inside RF shielded box or RF shielded room if the UE is not connected via cable.  26 Field setup: The UE is placed in the centre of cell close to the radiated eNB/gNB antenna(s), where excellent radio 27 conditions (SINR as defined in Section 3.6) should be observed. The minimum coupling loss (see Section 3.6) should not 28 be exceeded. 29 In both laboratory and field setups, Figure 5-6, the midhaul transport latency can be modified by using of a network 30 impairment emulator inserted between O-DU and O-CU. It is necessary to know/measure the transmission delays 31 produced by all midhaul transport components between O-DU and O-CU in order to properly estimate the total midhaul 32 latency. 33

The 9 utilization of these KPIs should be also verified.  10 4. The UE should receive the data from application server.  14 6. The midhaull latency is increased by 1000 us if no degradation of user peak downlink throughput was observer in the 18 previous measurement. Steps 5 to 8 are repeated until the total degradation of user peak downlink throughput is less than 30%. The KPIs 21 measured with the minim fronthaul latency are used as a baseline (100%) for calculation of the degradation. 22 10. [Optional] Steps 4 to 9 are repeated for downlink full-buffer TCP data transmission.   23

 Services 1 As a part of O-RAN testing, we need to ensure the O-RAN system can work in a telecom network, by inter-working with 2 the other sub-systems to provide End-to-End services. This section of the document outlines the services which needs to 3 be tested to validate that the O-RAN system can be deployed and optimized to deliver great end user service experience 4 in a telecom network. 5 The services which need to be tested are broadly classified as data service, video streaming service, voice service, video 6 calling service in the eMBB slice and services supported using other slices like URLLC and mMTC.  This section of the 7 document also tests different scenarios like handover and different radio conditions to assess the impact of these scenarios 8 on the end user service experience.   9 6.1 Data Services 10 Data services forms one of the basic services supported as of today on a telecom network. This includes everything 11 from web browsing, uploading/downloading content to traffic generated by all the different applications on the end user 12 device. These services form an integral part of data traffic on a telecom network. This section comprises of two test 13 scenarios which include web browsing and file upload/download. 14 Along with the monitoring and validation of these services using user experience KPIs, the O-RAN systems also need 15 to be monitored. The end user service experience can also be impacted by some of the features available on the O-RAN 16 system. Some of these features have been included below. As a part of the data services testing, details of these features 17 need to be included in the test report to get a comprehensive view of the setup used for testing. If additional features or 18 functionalities have been enabled during this testing that impact the end user experience, those need be included in the 19 test report as well.  20 • Control Channel Beamforming 21 • Connected Mode DRX 22 • Massive MIMO 23 • Coordinated Multi Point (DL and UL) 24 • 256QAM Support (DL and UL) 25 • SSB Power Boost 26 • Beam Management 27 • DL Common Channel Beamforming 28 • Single-User MIMO Beamforming (DL and UL) 29 • Multi-User MIMO Beamforming (DL and UL) 30 • Single-User MIMO, TM Switching 31 • Multi-User MIMO, TM Switching 32 • TDD configuration support 33 • RAN Slicing Framework (NR Low/Mid/High) 34 • RACH Enhancements (PRACH Format 0) 35 • PF (Proportional Fairness) Scheduling 36 • QoS Scheduling 37 • Minimal Bit Rate Scheduling 38

SA Test ID Data service         6.1.1 Web Browsing Service Y Y Y 6.1.2 File upload/download Service Y Y Y  6  7 6.1.1 Web Browsing 8 
6.1.1.1 Test Description 9 Web browsing forms an integral part of the 4G and 5G data network, and this test case is applicable to both NSA and 10 SA deployments. This testing will need to be performed twice, once for NSA and repeated again for SA deployment as 11 applicable. HTTP is the protocol used for web browsing and this protocol can be transported over TCP/TLS or 12 UDP/QUIC. As a part of this effort, we will include scenarios which will include both these protocols. Testing can be 13 performed using HTTP/TLS/TCP or HTTP/QUIC/UDP or both these protocols as applicable. 14 Web browsing KPI 15 • DNS Resolution time– Time taken from when the client sends a DNS query to when the DNS responds 16 with an IP address in milliseconds/seconds.  This KPI should be recorded if DNS is used. 17 • Time To First Byte (TTFB) – Time taken from when the client makes the HTTP request to when the first 18 byte of the page is received in milliseconds/seconds. 19 • Page Load Time – Time taken from when the client places the request to when the page is completely 20 loaded in seconds. 21 6.1.1.2 Test Setup 22 The SUT in this test case would be O-eNB along with O-RU, O-DU and O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP for NSA deployments or 23 just the O-RU, O-DU and O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP for SA deployments. The O-RAN setup should support the ability to 24 perform this testing in different radio conditions as defined in Section 3.6. The 4G/5G core will be required to support 25 the basic functionality to authenticate and register an end user device in order to setup a PDN connection/PDU session. 26 The 4G core will be used for O-RAN NSA testing, whereas 5G core will be used for O-RAN SA testing. The 4G/5G 27 core could be a completely emulated, partially emulated or a real non-emulated core. The application server should 28 support web browsing and be accessible from the 4G/5G core. This application server(s) should support protocols like 29 HTTP/TCP and/or HTTP/QUIC as applicable. The end user device (UE) used for testing could be a real UE or an 30 emulated one. The test setup should include tools which have the ability to collect traces on the elements and/or packet 31 captures of communication between the elements. This could be a built-in capability of the emulated/non-emulated 32 network elements and end user device or an external tool. Optionally, if some of the network elements or application 33 

  
_______________________________________________________________________________________________.                © 2022 by the O-RAN ALLIANCE e.V. Your use is subject to the copyright statement on the cover page of this specification. 103  
O-RAN.TIFG.E2E-Test.0-v04.00 O-RAN.TIFG.E2E-Test.0-v04.00 
server is located remotely either in a cloud or on the internet, the additional latency should be calculated and accounted 1 for.  2 The O-eNB, O-RU, O-DU and O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP need to have the right configuration and software load. Tools 3 which emulate latency need to be used and configured on the O-RAN system (between O-RU, O-DU and O-CU-CP/O-4 CU-UP) to emulate real-world deployment conditions as applicable. The end user device must be configured with the 5 right user credentials to be able to register and authenticate with the O-RAN system and the 4G/5G core. The end user 6 device also needs to be provisioned with the right application like the web browser to perform the tests. The 4G/5G core 7 network must be configured to support end user device used for testing. This includes supporting registration, 8 authentication and PDN connection/PDU session establishment for this end user device. The application server should 9 be configured to support web browsing, along with support for HTTP/TCP and/or HTTP/QUIC protocols as applicable. 10 Web browsing has lot of variables which can impact the KPIs and in order to reduce the variables and make the test 11 outcomes consistent, it is recommended to set up a static web page of ~1.8-2MB size. The size of the web page is 12 comparable to the average size of web pages on the internet. A static web page does not change the content on repeated 13 requests, thus making the test results consistent and repeatable. The locations where the radio conditions are excellent, 14 good, fair and poor need to be identified within the serving cell. 15 All the elements in the network like O-RAN system, 4G/5G core and the application server need to have the ability to 16 capture traces to validate the successful execution of the test cases. The end user device needs to have the capability to 17 capture traces/packet capture to calculate the web browsing KPIs. Optionally, the network could have network taps 18 deployed in various legs of the network to get packet captures to validate successful execution of the test cases. Finally, 19 all these different components need to have connectivity with each other – the end user device should be able to connect 20 to O-RAN system, which needs to be connected to the 4G/5G core which in turn should have connectivity to the 21 application server. 22 6.1.1.3 Test Methodology/Procedure 23 Ensure the end user device, O-RAN system, 4G/5G core and the application server have all been configured as outlined 24 in Section 6.1.1.2. All traces and packet captures need to be enabled for the duration of the testing to ensure all 25 communication between network elements can be captured and validated. 26 1. Power on the end user device in excellent radio condition and ensure it registers with the 4G/5G core for data 27 services. 28 2. Once the registration is complete, the end user device has to establish a PDU session with the 5G core (SA) or PDN 29 connection with the 4G core (NSA). 30 3. Open the browser or application on the end user device and start a web browsing session to a web content using 31 HTTP/TCP and/or HTTP/QUIC protocol. If the intent is to execute this test case for both protocols, then this step 32 needs to be performed twice, once for web content using HTTP/TCP followed by web content using HTTP/QUIC 33 protocol. 34 4. Validate the end user can get the content from the application server and view it on the end device. For every test 35 collect the KPIs included in Section 6.1.1.1. 36 5. Clear the browser cache on the end user device between tests.  37 6. Repeat the test multiple times (>10 times) and gather results. 38 7. Repeat the above steps 1 through 6 for the good, fair and poor radio conditions. 39  40 6.1.1.4 Test Expectation (expected results) 41 As a pre-validation, use the traces to validate a successful registration and PDN connection/PDU session setup by the 42 end user device without any errors. This is a prerequisite before these tests can be validated. 43

First and foremost, the end user device should be able to view the web browsing content for the web browsing test, with 1 the content being viewable and readable. 6.1.2.1 Test Description 6 File Transfer Protocol (FTP) is a simple application layer protocol used to transfer file between remote locations. FTP 7 along with different flavours of the protocols forms one of the fundamental methods to upload/download a file on the 8 internet. This test case is applicable to both NSA and SA deployments and will need to be performed twice, once for 9 NSA and repeated again for SA deployment as applicable. This scenario tests the end user experience to 10 upload/download files to/from an FTP server over an O-RAN system. The KPIs used to measure the user experience 11 have been included below 12 1. Download throughput – This is the average application layer throughput to download the file in kbps 13 2. Upload throughput – This is the average application layer throughput to upload the file in kbps. 14 3. Time taken to Download file – This is the time taken to download the file in seconds. 15 4. Time taken to Upload file – This is the time taken to upload the file in seconds. 16  17 6.1.2.2 Test Setup 18 The SUT in this test case would be O-eNB along with O-RU, O-DU and O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP for NSA deployments or 19 just the O-RU, O-DU and O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP for SA deployments. The O-RAN setup should support the ability to 20 perform this testing in different radio conditions as defined in Section 3.6. The 4G 22 core will be used for O-RAN NSA testing, whereas 5G core will be used for O-RAN SA testing. The 4G/5G core be a 23 completely emulated, partially emulated or a real non-emulated core. An FTP server acts as an application server for 24 this test case. The end user device (UE) used for testing could be a real UE or an emulated one. Delete the test files – downloaded file on the client and 31 the uploaded file on the FTP server. 32 6. Repeat the test multiple times (>10 times) and gather results. 33 7. The details of these features, along with any other features/functionality 2 which could impact the end user’s web browsing experience should be included in the test report to provide a 3 comprehensive view of the testing. 4 6.2 Video Streaming 5 Video makes up a major part of the internet traffic today and we are seeing similar trend even on the mobile data traffic. 6 Mobile video accounts for close to two-thirds of the total mobile data traffic and is expected to increase in the coming 7 years. Video streaming has evolved over time with newer and better audio and video codecs. The protocols used to 8 stream the video packets has also been evolving over time, with Adaptive Bit Rate (ABR) being the most common 9 protocol used for streaming video on the internet today. There are multiple flavours of ABR, but at the crux they all use 10 HTTP protocol over TCP/TLS or UPD/QUIC to transfer audio-video packets to a client. A video streaming server 11 which supports ABR hosts multiple versions of the same video content with each version of the video being encoded at 12 different resolution and quality, hence different bit rates. The client uses the ABR protocol to get the list of all available 13 versions of the requested video content and picks the best video content based on the available bandwidth on the client 14 side. The ABR client continuously monitors the network condition and dynamically adjusts the quality and resolution of 15 the video stream to match the available bandwidth. 16 The ABR protocol provides the user with the best video streaming experience based on the available bandwidth 17 between the client and the content server. This however adds a lot of variables to the streaming session 18 • An ABR client may start with a lower resolution video and progressively switch to higher resolution video as it 19 better estimates the bandwidth available at the client. 20 • An ABR client might notice an improvement in the bandwidth and request a higher resolution/quality video 21 content, thus improving the video quality mid-stream. 22 • An ABR client might notice a degradation in the bandwidth and request a lower resolution/quality video 23 content, thus deteriorating the video quality mid-stream. 24  25 These variables make it challenging to quantify the video streaming experience of an end user. There have been many 26 tools and organization which have defined different methods and algorithms to quantify the end user experience 27 including QoE (Quality of Experience), SVQ (Streaming Video Quality), Video Multimethod Assessment Fusion 28 (VMAF) etc. In this document we recommend using the Mean Opinion Score (MOS) as defined by ITU P.1203.3 to 29 quantify the quality of experience of the end user. This mechanism however limits the testing to H264 video encoder 30 with a resolution of HD quality (1080p resolution – 1920 x 1080 pixels) or below. 31 Video Streaming KPIs 32 • Video start time or Time to load first video frame – Time from when the video was selected to play to 33 when the video starts playing in seconds. 34 

  
_______________________________________________________________________________________________.                © 2022 by the O-RAN ALLIANCE e.V. Your use is subject to the copyright statement on the cover page of this specification. 109  
O-RAN.TIFG.E2E-Test.0-v04.00 O-RAN.TIFG.E2E-Test.0-v04.00 
• Number of video stalls/buffering (Optional) – Number of times the video stalled or started buffering during 1 the course of video streaming. This KPI has already been considered by ITU P.1203.3 to provide a 2 cumulative MOS score. 3 • Duration of stalls in the video (Optional) – The cumulative duration of all the stalls during the course of 4 video streaming in seconds. This KPI has already been considered by ITU P.1203.3 to provide a 5 cumulative MOS score. 6 • Video MOS score – MOS score for the video streaming session as defined by ITU P1203.3. 7  8 Along with the monitoring and validation of these services using user experience KPIs, the O-RAN systems also need 9 to be monitored. Optionally, if some of the network elements or application server(s) are located 18 remotely either in a cloud or on the internet, the additional latency should be calculated and accounted for.  19 The O-eNB needs to have the right configuration and software load. The pair of O-RUs (O-RU1 and O-RU2) need to be 20 connected to the O-DU and O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP and all the components need to have the right configuration and 21 software load. Tools which emulate latency need to be used and configured on the O-RAN system (between O-RU, O-22 DU and O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP) to emulate real-world deployment conditions. The end user device must be configured 23 with the right user credentials to be able to register and authenticate with the O-RAN system and the 4G core. The end 24 user device also needs to be provisioned with the right application like a video streaming client to perform the tests. The 25 4G core network must be configured to support end user device used for testing. This includes supporting registration, 26 authentication and PDN connection establishment for this end user device. 27 All the elements in the network like O-RAN system, 4G core and the application server need to have the ability to 28 capture traces to validate the successful execution of the test cases. The end user device needs to have the capability to 29 capture traces/packet capture to calculate the video streaming KPIs. Optionally, the network could have network taps 30 deployed in various legs of the network to get packet captures to validate successful execution of the test cases. Finally, 31 all these different components need to have connectivity with each other – the end user device should be able to connect 32 to O-RAN system(O-eNB, O-RUs, O-DU and O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP), O-RAN system needs to be connected to the 4G 33 core which in turn should have connectivity to the application server. 34 6.2.2.3 Test Methodology/Procedure 35 Ensure the end user device, O-RAN system, 4G core and the application server have all been configured as outlined in 36 Section 6.2.2.2. All traces and packet captures need to be enabled for the duration of the testing to ensure all 37 communication between network elements can be captured and validated. 38 1. Power on the end user device and ensure it registers with the 4G core for data services over NSA by connecting 39 over the O-eNB as Master eNB and O-RU1 of the O-RAN system as secondary gNB. 40 2. Once the registration is complete, the end user device has to establish a PDN connection with the 4G core. 41 3. Open the video streaming client on the end user device and start a streaming session over HTTP/TCP protocol.  42 4. Once the video streaming session has started, move the device so it can handover from O-RU1 to O-RU2 on the 43 Secondary gNB while it continues to use the O-eNB as the Master eNB.  44

These values are applicable when the testing 14 is performed in a controlled environment in good radio condition without the interference of external factors which 15 could impact the KPIs, example: use of internet to connect to remote servers/hosts could add latency, jitter and packet 16 loss issues to the connection, thus impacting the KPIs. Any test results outside 18 the range of KPI encountered during testing, will have to be investigated to identify the root cause as the issues may be 19 due to the variables outside the SUT. This scenario tests the end user’s video streaming experience when the end user device is connected over 15 NSA to the 4G core and performs a handover between O-RUs which are connected to different O-DUs, which in turn 16 are connected to the same O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP on the Secondary gNB – Inter-O-DU Intra-O-CU handover. The end 17 user device remains connected to the same Master eNB through the entire handover process. This handover is agnostic 18 to the 4G core as the handover occurs on the O-RAN system. This test assesses the impact of the video streaming 19 service on the end user device in this handover scenario by monitoring the KPIs included in Section 6.2 This test case 20 streams video using ABR protocol over HTTP/TCP and/or HTTP/QUIC.  21

_______________________________________________________________________________________________.                © 2022 by the O-RAN ALLIANCE e.V. Your use is subject to the copyright statement on the cover page of this specification. 124

provided using a packet switched network. This Section of the document includes test case to validate the Voice over 1 LTE service in different scenarios. The KPIs which will be monitored to assess the voice service are included below 2 • CSSR – Call Setup Success Rate % – Total number of calls which were successful by the total number of 3 calls made as a percentage. 4 • CST – Call Setup Time – Time taken from the initial SIP INVITE to when the SIP 180 Ringing is received 5 in seconds. 6 • MOS Score – Mean Opinion Score for the voice call. This needs to be measured on both ends of the Voice 7 call – mobile originated and mobile terminated. 8 • Mute Rate – This is the percentage of calls which were muted in both directions (calls with RTP loss of > 9 3-4s in both directions are considered muted call). This needs to be measured on both ends of the voice call 10 – mobile originated & mobile terminated and counted only once per call. 11 • One Way Calls – This is the percentage of calls which were muted in any one direction (calls with RTP 12 loss of > 3-4s in one direction only are considered one-way calls). This needs to be monitored on both ends 13 of the voice call – mobile originated & mobile terminated and counted only once per call. 14 • RTP Packet Loss % - Number of RTP packets which were dropped/lost in uplink/downlink direction as a 15 percentage of total packets. This needs to be measured on both ends of the voice call – mobile originated 16 and mobile terminated. 17  18 Along with the monitoring and validation of these services using user experience KPIs, the O-RAN systems also need 19 to be monitored. The end user service experience can also be impacted by some of the features available on the O-RAN 20 system. Some of these features have been included below. As a part of the voice services testing, details of these 21 features need to be included in the test report to get a comprehensive view of the setup used for testing. If additional 22 features or functionalities have been enabled during this testing that impact the end user voice experience, those need be 23 included in the test report as well.  24 • RLC in Unacknowledged Mode 25 • Robust Header Compression 26 • DRX 27 • Dynamic GBR Admission Control 28 • TTI Bundling 29 • VoLTE Inactivity Timer 30 • Frequency Hopping 31 • Multi-Target RRC Connection Re-Establishment 32 • VoLTE HARQ 33 • Coordinated Multi Point (DL and UL) 34 • VoLTE Quality Enhancement 35 • Packet Loss Detection 36 • Voice Codec Aware scheduler 37 • NR to LTE PS Redirection/Cell Reselection/Handover 38 • LTE to NR PS Redirection/Cell Reselection/Handover 39 Please see below summary table of test cases and applicable technology.  40 Table 6-10: VoLTE Test Case Selection summary 41   Applicable technology

SA Test ID Voice service (VoLTE)         6.3.1 VoLTE Stationary Test Service Y N/A N/A 6.3.2 VoLTE handover Test (intra) Service Y N/A N/A 6.3.3 Voice Service – LTE to NR / NR to LTE handover test Service Y N/A Y 6.3.1 VoLTE Stationary Test 1 This scenario tests the voice service experience on an LTE network – Voice over LTE (VoLTE) when the end user 2 device is stationary.  3 6.3.1.1 Test Description 4 With penetration of LTE, VoLTE has become the primary method of providing voice service. VoLTE uses IP packets 5 to send and receive voice packets, with the IP packets being transferred over LTE. IP Multimedia System (IMS) forms 6 an important part of VoLTE as it is used to setup control and data plane needed for VoLTE communication. As voice 7 service is latency sensitive, the 4G core interacts with the IMS core to setup different bearers for Voice traffic – QCI-5 8 for VoLTE control plane and QCI-1 for VoLTE data plane. This test case is applicable when UE is connected over an 9 O-RAN system to a 4G core in an NSA deployment. 10 6.3.1.2 Test Setup 11 The SUT in this test case would be a O-eNB which would be the Master eNB and a Secondary gNB. As most of the 12 current NSA deployment use the 4G eNB to provide voice services, the use of a secondary gNB is optional. We have 13 however included the Secondary gNB in this test scenario, but it is only applicable if the gNB plays a role in 14 establishing the control plane or data plane for a voice call. The O-eNB, gNB and the components within these have to 15 comply with the O-RAN specifications. The O-RAN setup should support the ability to perform this testing in different 16 radio conditions as defined in Section 3.6. A 4G core will be required to support the basic functionality to authenticate 17 and register an end user device in order to setup a PDN connection. An IMS core will be required to register the end 18 user device to support voice services on a 4G network. The 4G and IMS cores could be a completely emulated, partially 19 emulated or real non-emulated cores. We will need at least two end user devices (UE) which can be a real UEs or an 20 emulated one, and both have to support voice service using VoLTE. The end user devices will serve as Mobile 21 Originated (MO) and Mobile Terminated (MT) end user devices forming the two ends of the voice call. Going forward 22 in this section, these end user devices will be referred to as MO end user device and MT end user devices to represent 23 the role they plan in the voice call. The test setup should include tools which have the ability to collect traces on the 24 elements and/or packet captures of communication between the elements. This could be a built-in capability of the 25 emulated/non-emulated network elements or an external tool. Optionally, if some of the network elements are located 26 remotely either in a cloud or on the internet, the additional latency should be calculated and accounted for.  27 The Master eNB, Secondary gNB and their components (O-eNB, O-RU, O-DU and O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP) need to have 28 the right configuration and software load. The end user device must be configured with the right user credentials to be 29 able to register and authenticate with the O-RAN system and the 4G core. The end user devices also need to be 30 provisioned with the user credentials to attach to the 4G core and register with the IMS core to perform voice call using 31 VoLTE. The 4G core network and IMS core must be configured to support end user devices used for testing. This 32 includes supporting registration, authentication and PDN connection establishment for these end user devices. This also 33 includes provisioning the IMS core to support registration of the end user devices to make voice calls over VoLTE, and 34 dynamically setting up dedicated bearers for voice calls. The locations where the radio conditions are excellent, good, 35 fair and poor need to be identified within the serving cell. 36

This test scenario is applicable for a 5G SA deployment. 7 6.3.3.1 Test Description 8 Voice service is one of the basic services provided on the telecommunication network. Voice service on the 4G network 9 is provided using VoLTE. Similarly, voice service on the 5G network is provided using packet switch technology called 10 Voice over New Radio (VoNR). As 5G network is being deployed by a telecommunication service provider, the service 11 provider will need to support 4G and 5G network, and thus support VoLTE, VoNR and the handover between the two 12 voice services. This scenario tests the end user’s voice experience when the end user device performs a handover from 13 VoLTE to VoNR and vice versa. 14 6.3.3.2 Test Setup 15 The SUT in this test case would be an O-eNB along with a gNB (O-RU, O-DU and O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP). We would 16 also need an interworking 4G-5G core (referred to as 4G-5G core going forward) which supports a combined anchor 17 point for 4G and 5G, i.e. SMF+PGW-C and UPF+PGW-U. The eNB connects to a 4G-5G core over the 4G interfaces 18 like S1 to provide 4G LTE service and the O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP, O-DU and O-RU would connect to a 4G-5G core over 19 the 5G interfaces like N2 and N3 to provide 5G SA service. The O-eNB, gNB and the components within these have to 20 comply with the O-RAN specifications. The 4G-5G core will be required to support the basic functionality to 21 authenticate and register an end user device in order to setup a PDN connection/PDU session. An IMS core will be 22 required to register the end user device to support voice services on a 4G and 5G network. The 4G and 5G core have to 23 interwork using the N26 interface between the MME and AMF to support seamless handover. We are recommending 24 the use of N26 interface to ensure better customer experience when the end user device performs a handover between 25 4G and 5G. The 4G-5G and IMS cores could be a completely emulated, partially emulated or real non-emulated cores. 26 We will need at least two end user devices (UE) which can be a real UEs or an emulated one, and both have to support 27 voice service using VoLTE, VoNR and the capability to handover from VoLTE to VoNR and vice versa. The end user 28 devices will serve as Mobile Originated (MO) and Mobile Terminated (MT) end user devices forming the two ends of 29 the voice call. Going forward in this section, these end user devices will be referred to as MO end user device and MT 30 end user devices to represent their role in the voice call. The test setup should include tools which have the ability to 31 collect traces on the elements and/or packet captures of communication between the elements. This could be a built-in 32 capability of the emulated/non-emulated network elements or an external tool. Optionally, if some of the network 33 elements are located remotely either in a cloud or on the internet, the additional latency should be calculated and 34 accounted for.  35 The O-eNB, gNB and their components (O-RU, O-DU and O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP) need to have the right configuration 36 and software load. The end user devices must be configured with the right user credentials to be able to register and 37 authenticate with the O-RAN system and the 4G-5G core. The end user devices also need to be provisioned with the 38 user credentials to support attach procedure to the 4G-5G core, registering to the IMS core and performing a voice call 39 using VoLTE and VoNR. This includes supporting registration, authentication and PDN connection/PDU Session 40 establishment for these end user devices. This also includes provisioning the IMS core to support registration of the end 41

The details of these features, along with any other 2 features/functionality which could impact the end user’s voice service experience should be included in the test report to 3 provide a comprehensive view of the testing. 4 6.4 Voice Service – EPS Fallback 5 Voice service is one of the basic services which must be supported on every telecom network. Upgrade of telecom 6 network occurs in phases, and this is no different for 5G. The telecom network may not be able to support voice service 7 on 5G during this phase for multiple reasons – the 5G network may not be deployed nationwide, or 5G network may not 8 be tuned to support voice service or the devices may not be able to support voice service on 5G. However, there can be 9 no interruption to voice service during this transition phase. EPS fallback is the method used to support voice services 10 during this phase, where voice services are continued to be supported on the legacy LTE network using VoLTE by 11 forcing the device to fallback to LTE to make or receive a call. The KPIs which will be monitored to assess the voice 12 service are included below 13 • CSSR – Call Setup Success Rate % – Total number of calls which were successful by the total number of 14 calls made as a percentage. 15 • CST – Call Setup Time – Time taken from the initial SIP INVITE to when the SIP 180 Ringing is received 16 in seconds. 17 • MOS Score – Mean Opinion Score for the voice call. This needs to be measured on both ends of the Voice 18 call – mobile originated and mobile terminated. 19 • Mute Rate % – This is the percentage of calls which were muted in both directions (calls with RTP loss of 20 > 3-4s in both directions are considered muted call). This needs to be measured on both ends of the Voice 21 call – mobile originated and mobile terminated and counted only once per call. 22 • One Way Calls % – This is the percentage of calls which were muted in any one direction (calls with RTP 23 loss of > 3-4s in one direction only are considered one-way calls). This needs to be monitored on both ends 24 of the Voice call – mobile originated and mobile terminated and counted only once per call. 25 • RTP Packet Loss % - Number of RTP packets which were dropped/lost in uplink/downlink direction as a 26 percentage of total packets. This needs to be measured on both ends of the Voice call – mobile originated 27 and mobile terminated. 28  29 Along with the monitoring and validation of these services using user experience KPIs, the O-RAN systems also need 30 to be monitored. The end user service experience can also be impacted by some of the features available on the O-RAN 31

This testing only applies to 5G SA deployment and in this scenario 31 the UE is connected to the 5G SA Core and registered with the IMS core for voice service. When the end user device 32 

  
_______________________________________________________________________________________________.                © 2022 by the O-RAN ALLIANCE e.V. Your use is subject to the copyright statement on the cover page of this specification. 133  
O-RAN.TIFG.E2E-Test.0-v04.00 O-RAN.TIFG.E2E-Test.0-v04.00 
(UE) wants to make a voice call or receive a voice call, the network informs the device to fallback to the LTE network 1 to make/receive the voice call. The EPS fallback does increase the Call Setup Time due to the time needed to fallback 2 before making/receiving the voice call.  3 There are two mechanism in which EPS fallback is supported on the 5G core, and the methodology used impacts the 4 time taken to perform the fallback to LTE, and hence impacts the Call Setup Time. The two mechanisms that could be 5 used to perform EPS fallback is included below. These two mechanisms do change the test setup but does not change 6 the testing procedure. 7 • With N26 interface – The AMF in the 5G core communicates with the MME in the 4G over the N26 interface. 8 In this scenario the UE performs a handover from the 5G network to the 4G network. 9 • Without N26 interface – The AMF in the 5G core does not communicate directly with the 4G core, instead 10 uses the UDM/HSS to store and transfer relevant session information to the 4G core. In this scenario the UE 11 performs a Release with Redirect from the 5G network to the 4G network. 12 6.4.1.2 Test Setup 13 The SUT in this test case would be an O-eNB along with a gNB (O-RU, O-DU and O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP). We would 14 also need an interworking 4G-5G core (referred to as 4G-5G core going forward) which supports a combined anchor 15 point, i.e. SMF+PGW-C and UPF+PGW-U. The O-eNB connects to a 4G-5G core over the 4G interfaces like S1 to 16 provide 4G LTE service and the gNB (O-RU, O-DU and O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP) would connect to the 4G-5G core over 17 the 5G interfaces like N2 and N3 to provide 5G SA service. The O-eNB, gNB and the components within these have to 18 comply with the O-RAN specifications and support EPS fallback. The 4G-5G core will be required to support the basic 19 functionality to authenticate and register an end user device in order to setup a PDN connection/PDU session. The IMS 20 core will be required to register the end user device and needs to be integrated with the 4G-5G core to support voice 21 services over VoLTE and EPS fallback. The 4G-5G core have to interwork either using the N26 interface or without 22 using N26 interface depending on the desired core network configuration. The existence of the N26 interface does 23 reduce the EPS fallback time, hence reduce Call Setup Time and provide better end user experience. The 4G-5G and 24 IMS cores could be a completely emulated, partially emulated or real non-emulated cores. We will need at least two end 25 user devices (UE) which can be a real UEs or an emulated one, and both have to support voice service using VoLTE 26 and EPS fallback procedure. The end user devices will serve as Mobile Originated (MO) and Mobile Terminated (MT) 27 end user devices forming the two ends of the voice call. For the sake of clarity, we will address these end user devices 28 as UE-1 and UE-2 in this section. The test setup should include tools which have the ability to collect traces on the 29 elements and/or packet captures of communication between the elements. This could be a built-in capability of the 30 emulated/non-emulated network elements or an external tool. Optionally, if some of the network elements are located 31 remotely either in a cloud or on the internet, the additional latency should be calculated and accounted for.  32 The O-eNB, gNB and their components (O-RU, O-DU and O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP) need to have the right configuration 33 and software load. The end user device must be configured with the right user credentials to be able to register and 34 authenticate with the O-RAN system and the 4G-5G core. The end user devices also need to be provisioned with the 35 user credentials to attach to the 4G-5G core and register with the IMS core to perform voice call using VoLTE and EPS 36 fallback. The 4G-5G core network and IMS core must be configured to support voice service on the end user devices 37 used for testing, which includes dynamically setting up dedicated bearers for voice calls.  38 All the elements in the network like O-RAN system, 4G-5G core and the IMS Core need to have the ability to capture 39 traces to validate the successful execution of the test cases. The end user devices need to have the capability to capture 40 traces/packet capture to calculate the VoLTE KPIs. Optionally, the network could have network taps deployed in 41 various legs of the network to get packet captures to validate successful execution of the test cases. Finally, all these 42 different components need to have connectivity with each other – the end user device should be able to connect to O-43 RAN system(O-eNBs and gNBs), O-RAN system needs to be connected to the 4G-5G core which in turn should have 44 connectivity to the IMS Core. 45

6.4.1.3 Test Methodology/Procedure 1 Ensure the end user devices, O-RAN system, 4G-5G core and the IMS Core have all been configured as outlined in 2 Section 6.4.1.2. In this test scenario, one of the end user device is going to be connected over the 4G O-eNB to the 4G-3 5G core, while the other end user device is going to be connected over 5G gNB (O-RU, O-DU and O-CU-CP/O-CU-4 UP) to the 4G-5G core. All traces and packet captures need to be enabled for the duration of the testing to ensure all 5 communication between network elements can be captured and validated. 6 1. Power on the two end user devices and ensure UE-1 is in LTE coverage, when UE-2 is in the 5G coverage. 7 Validate both end user devices register to the 4G-5G core. 8 2. Once the registration is complete, UE-1 and UE-2 have to establish a PDN connection and PDU session 9 respectively with the 4G-5G core. Once the PDN connection and PDU session have been setup, both the end user 10 devices have to register with the IMS core to support voice services. 11 3. Use UE-1 as the MO end user device to call UE-2. Validate the UE-2 performs EPS fallback to LTE to receive the 12 call.  13 4. Answer the call on UE-2 and continue to have two-way communication for at least 5 minutes and terminate the 14 call. Measure the voice KPIs included in Section 6.4.  15 5. At this point UE-2 should have completed the call and moved back to connect to the 4G-5G Core over 5G gNB. 16 6. Use UE-2 as the MO end user device to call UE-1. Validate the UE-2 performs EPS fallback to LTE to before 17 making the call.  18 7. Answer the call on UE-1 and continue to have two-way communication for at least 5 minutes and terminate the 19 call. Measure the voice KPIs included in Section 6.4.  20 8. Repeat the test multiple times (>10 times) and gather results. 21  22 6.4.1.4 Test Expectation (expected results) 23 As a pre-validation, use the traces to validate end user device UE-1 is able to perform a successful registration and PDN 24 connection setup while using the O-eNB in LTE coverage. Similarly, use the traces to validate end user device UE-2 is 25 able to perform a successful registration and PDU session setup while using gNB in 5G coverage. Also validate both the 26 end user devices are able to register with the IMS core for voice services. This is a prerequisite before these tests can be 27 validated. 28 Validate the end user devices are able to make a voice call between each other by dynamically setting up a QCI-1 bearer 29 to transfer voice packets over RTP. Validate the device which is in the 5G coverage falls back to 4G before receiving or 30 making a voice call, in other words the EPS fallback procedure has been executed successfully. The Call Setup Time 31 will be higher than VoLTE due to the delay associated with executing the EPS fallback procedure. Even though this test 32 case does not directly impact the quality of the voice call, for the sake of consistency, ensure the voice quality from both 33 parties are clear and audible without one-way or intermittent muting. Use the packet captures to validate there is no 34 RTP packet drops or high RTP packet jitter which could cause voice muting issues. Use the packet captures to ensure 35 there are no out-of-sequence packets which could impact customer’s voice experience.  36 The average range of values for the KPIs are included below for guidance. These values are applicable when the testing 37 is performed in a controlled environment in good radio condition without the interference of external factors which 38 could impact the KPIs, example: use of internet to connect to remote network nodes could add latency, jitter and packet 39 loss issues to the connection, thus impacting the KPIs. As there are multiple variables which can impact the testing in 40 this scenario, a KPI outcome outside the range does not necessarily point to a failed test case. Any test results outside 41 the range of KPI encountered during testing, will have to be investigated to identify the root cause as the issues may be 42 due to the variables outside the SUT. If the root cause is not in the SUT, then the issues have to be resolved and the test 43 has to be repeated.  44

VoNR also packetizes voice and uses IP packets for 6 voice communication. On the Core and IMS side, VoNR is very similar to VoLTE. VoNR also uses IMS system for 7 voice service, and the IMS interacts with the 5G core to setup separate bearer for voice service. This section tests the 8 voice service over VoNR in different scenarios. The KPIs which will be monitored to assess the voice service are 9 included below 10 • CSSR – Call Setup Success Rate % – Total number of calls which were successful by the total number of 11 calls made as a percentage. 12 • CST – Call Setup Time – Time taken from the initial SIP INVITE to when the SIP 180 Ringing is received 13 in seconds. 14 • MOS Score – Mean Opinion Score for the voice call. This needs to be monitored on both ends 21 of the Voice call – mobile originated and mobile terminated and counted only once per call. 22 • RTP Packet Loss % - Number of RTP packets which were dropped/lost in uplink/downlink direction as a 23 percentage of total packets. This needs to be measured on both ends of the Voice call – mobile originated 24 and mobile terminated. 25  26 End user voice service experience can also be impacted by some of the features available on the O-RU, O-DU and O-27 CU-CP/O-CU-UP. VoNR is similar to VoLTE where it uses IP packets to 14 send and receive voice packets, with the IP packets being transferred over NR or 5G radio. Just like VoLTE, IMS is 15 used to setup the control and data plane for the voice communication. As voice service is latency sensitive, the 5G core 16 interacts with the IMS core to setup different QoS flows for voice traffic – 5QI-5 for VoNR control plane and 5QI-1 for 17 VoNR data plane. This test case is applicable when end user device (UE) is connected over a 5G SA network.  18 6.5.1.2 Test Setup 19 The SUT in this test case would be a gNB (O-RU, O-DU and O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP) which is used to test the voice 20 service. We 23 will need at least two end user devices (UE) which can be a real UEs or an emulated one, and both have to support 24 voice service using VoNR. The end user devices will serve as Mobile Originated (MO) and Mobile Terminated (MT) 25 end user devices forming the two ends of the voice call. Going forward in this section, these end user devices will be 26 referred to as MO end user device and MT end user device to represent their role in the voice call. The test setup should 27 include tools which have the ability to collect traces on the elements and/or packet captures of communication between 28 the elements. The details of these features, along with any other features/functionality which 2 could impact the end user’s voice service experience should be included in the test report to provide a comprehensive 3 view of the testing. 4 6.5.2 VoNR – Intra-Distributed Unit (O-DU) handover 5 This test scenario validates the user’s voice experience when the end user device (UE) is on a VoNR call and performs a 6 handover between two O-RUs which connect to the same O-DU (Intra-O-DU handover) 7 6.5.2.1 Test Description 8 The 5G O-RAN system has multiple sub-components like the O-RU, O-DU and the O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP. This setup 9 leads to multiple handover scenarios between the O-RAN sub-components. This particular scenario tests the end user’s 10 voice experience during the handover between two O-RUs which are connected to the same O-DU (and O-CU-CP/O-11 CU-UP), hence an Intra-O-DU handover. This handover will be agnostic to the 5G core as the handover occurs on the 12 O-RAN system. This test assesses the impact on the end user’s voice service in this handover scenario by monitoring 13 the KPIs included in Section 6.5. 14 6.5.2.2 Test Setup 15 The SUT in this test case would be a pair of O-RUs which connect to the same O-DU and O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP (refer 16 Section 4.4). This O-RAN setup is used to test the voice service during a handover. A 5G SA Core would be required to 17 support basic functionality to authenticate and register the end user device to establish a PDU session. An IMS core will 18 be required to register the end user device to support voice services on a 5G network. The 5G and IMS cores could be a 19 completely emulated, partially emulated or real non-emulated cores. We will need at least two end user devices (UE) 20 which can be a real UEs or an emulated one, and both have to support voice service using 5G – Voice over NR(VoNR). 21 The end user devices will serve as Mobile Originated (MO) and Mobile Terminated (MT) end user devices forming the 22 two ends of the voice call. Going forward in this section, these end user devices will be referred to as MO end user 23 device and MT end user device to represent their role in the voice call. The test setup should include tools which have 24 the ability to collect traces on the elements and/or packet captures of communication between the elements. This could 25 be a built-in capability of the emulated/non-emulated network elements or an external tool. Optionally, if some of the 26 network elements are located remotely either in a cloud or on the internet, the additional latency should be calculated 27 and accounted for.   28 The pair of O-RUs (O-RU1 and O-RU2) need to be connected to the O-DU and O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP and all the 29 components need to have the right configuration and software load. The end user devices must be configured with the 30 right user credentials to be able to register and authenticate with the O-RAN system and the 5G core. The end user 31 devices also need to be provisioned with the user credentials to register and setup PDU session with the 5G core and 32 register with the IMS core to perform voice call using VoNR. The 5G core network and IMS core must be configured to 33 support voice service on the end user devices used for testing, which includes the ability to dynamically set up QoS 34 Flows for voice calls. 35 All the elements in the network like O-RAN system, 5G core and the IMS Core need to have the ability to capture 36 traces to validate the successful execution of the test cases. The end user devices need to have the capability to capture 37 traces/packet capture to calculate the VoNR KPIs. Optionally, the network could have network taps deployed in various 38

legs of the network to get packet captures to validate successful execution of the test cases. Finally, all these different 1 components need to have connectivity with each other – the end user device should be able to connect to O-RAN 2 system(O-RUs connected to O-DU and O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP), O-RAN system needs to be connected to the 5G core 3 which in turn should have connectivity to the IMS Core. 4 6.5.2.3 Test Methodology/Procedure 5 Ensure the end user devices, O-RAN system, 5G core and the IMS Core have all been configured as outlined in Section 6 6.5.2.2. In this test scenario, both the mobile originated and mobile terminated end user devices are going to use the 7 same O-RAN system, 5G and IMS core to perform the end-to-end voice call. All traces and packet captures need to be 8 enabled for the duration of the testing to ensure all communication between network elements can be captured and 9 validated. 10 1. Power on the two end user devices and ensure both of the devices registers with the 5G core for voice services over 11 SA by connecting over the O-RAN (O-RU, O-DU and O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP). Ensure both the MO & MT end user 12 devices are in the coverage area of the same O-RU – O-RU1. 13 2. Once the registration is complete, the MO and MT end user devices have to establish PDU session with the 5G 14 core. Once the PDU session has been setup, both the end user devices have to register with the IMS core to support 15 voice services. 16 3. Use the MO end user device to call the MT end user device. Validate the MT end user device is able to receive and 17 answer the call. 18 4. Once the call has been setup, move the MO end user device from the coverage area of O-RU1 to coverage area of 19 O-RU2, thus causing a handover from O-RU1 to O-RU2.  20 5. Continue the two-way voice communication between MO and MT end user devices until the handover procedure is 21 complete before terminating the voice call.  22 6. Repeat the test (steps 1 through 5) multiple times (> 10 times) and gather results. 23  24 6.5.2.4 Test Expectation (expected results) 25 As a pre-validation, use the traces to validate a successful registration and PDU session setup by the end user devices 26 without any errors over O-RU, O-DU and O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP. Also validate both the end user devices are able to 27 register with the IMS core for voice services. This is a prerequisite before these tests can be validated. 28 Validate the end user devices are able to make a voice call between each other by dynamically setting up a 5QI-1 bearer 29 to transfer voice packets over RTP. Ensure the Call Setup Time is reasonable, the voice quality from both parties are 30 clear and audible without one-way or intermittent muting through the duration of the call, especially during the 31 handover process. Use the packet captures to validate there is no RTP packet drops or high RTP packet jitter which 32 could cause voice muting issues. Use the packet captures to ensure there are no out-of-sequence packets which could 33 impact customer’s voice experience.  34 The average range of values for the KPIs are included below for guidance. These values are applicable when the testing 35 is performed in a controlled environment in good radio condition without the interference of external factors which 36 could impact the KPIs, example: use of internet to connect to remote servers/hosts could add latency, jitter and 37 reliability issues to the connection, thus impacting the KPIs. As there are multiple variables which can impact the 38 testing in this scenario, a KPI outcome outside the range does not necessarily point to a failed test case. Any test results 39 outside the range of KPI encountered during testing, will have to be investigated to identify the root cause as the issues 40 may be due to the variables outside the SUT. If the root cause is not in the SUT, then the issues have to be resolved and 41 the test has to be repeated.  42 • CSSR – Call Setup Success Rate % –> 99%. 43 • CST – Call Setup Time – < 2.5s 44

This handover occurs on the O-RAN system 5 and the 5G core is aware of the handover as it needs to send data to a new O-CU as a part of the handover process. This 6 test assesses the impact on the end user’s voice service in this handover scenario by monitoring the KPIs included in 7 Section 6.5. 8 6.5.4.2 Test Setup 9 The SUT in this test case would be a pair of O-RAN subsystems, a set of O-RU, O-DU and O-CU which interconnects 10 with another set of O-RU, O-DU and O-CU (refer Section 4.6). The details of these features, along with any other features/functionality which could impact 8 the end user’s voice service experience should be included in the test report to provide a comprehensive view of the 9 testing. 10 6.6 Video Service – Video over LTE (ViLTE) 11 Voice service has been one of the basic services provided on the mobile device since launch. With increasing LTE 12 penetration and the availability of higher speeds, video calls are slowly replacing voice calls. With the launch of 5G 13 which promises much higher throughput, the shift towards using video calls is only going to get faster. This section of 14 the document validates the user’s video calling experience in different conditions on the LTE network. This section of 15 the document only applies to the video calling service provided by the telecom service provider, which uses the telecom 16 operator’s IMS core to establish dedicated bearer to provide superior video calling experience. The KPIs which will be 17 monitored to assess the voice service are included below 18 • CSSR – Call Setup Success Rate % – Total number of calls which were successful by the total number of 19 calls made as a percentage. 20

• CST – Call Setup Time – Time taken from the initial SIP INVITE to when the SIP 180 Ringing is received 1 in seconds. 2 • MOS Score – Mean Opinion Score for the video call. This needs to be measured on both ends of the video 3 call – mobile originated and mobile terminated. 4 • Mute Rate % – This is the percentage of video calls which were muted or video freezes in both directions 5 (calls with RTP loss of > 3-4s in both directions are considered muted call). This needs to be measured on 6 both ends of the video call – mobile originated and mobile terminated and counted only once per call. 7 • One Way Calls % – This is the percentage of video calls which were muted, or video is not transmitted in 8 any one direction only (video calls with RTP loss of > 3-4s in one direction only are considered one-way 9 calls). This needs to be monitored on both ends of the Voice call – mobile originated and mobile terminated 10 and counted only once per call. 11 • RTP Packet Loss % - Number of RTP packets which were dropped/lost in uplink/downlink direction as a 12 percentage of total packets. This needs to be measured on both ends of the Voice call – mobile originated 13 and mobile terminated. 14  15 Along with the monitoring and validation of these services using user experience KPIs, the O-RAN systems also need 16 to be monitored. Video over LTE (ViLTE) uses the same mechanism as VoLTE, i.e. IP packets to send and receive 8 video & audio packets, with the IP packets being transferred over LTE. Just like VoLTE, IMS is used to setup control 9 and data plane needed for ViLTE communication. As two-way video service is latency sensitive, the EPC core interacts 10 with the IMS core to setup different bearers for Video traffic – QCI-5 for ViLTE control plane and QCI-2 for ViLTE 11 data plane. This test case is applicable when UE is connected over an NSA network. This section tests ViLTE user 12 experience on an O-RAN system. 13 6.6.1.2 Test Setup 14 The SUT in this test case would be a O-eNB which would the Master eNB and a Secondary gNB. As most of the 15 current NSA deployment use the 4G eNB to provide video calling services, the use of a secondary gNB is optional. We 16 will however include the Secondary gNB in this test scenario, but it is only applicable if the gNB plays a role in 17 establishing the control plane or data plane for a video call. The O-eNB, gNB and the components within these have to 18 comply with the O-RAN specifications. The O-RAN setup should support the ability to perform this testing in different 19 radio conditions as defined in Section 3.6. A 4G core will be required to support the basic functionality to authenticate 20 and register an end user device in order to setup a PDN connection. An IMS core will be required to register the end 21 user device to support video calling services on a 4G network. The 4G and IMS cores could be a completely emulated, 22 partially emulated or real non-emulated cores. We will need at least two end user devices (UE) which can be a real UEs 23 or an emulated one, and both have to support video calling service using ViLTE. The end user devices will serve as 24 Mobile Originated (MO) and Mobile Terminated (MT) end user devices forming the two ends of the video call Going 25 forward in this section, these end user devices will be referred to as MO end user device and MT end user devices to 26 represent the role they plan in the video call. The test setup should include tools which have the ability to collect traces 27 on the elements and/or packet captures of communication between the elements. This could be a built-in capability of 28 the emulated/non-emulated network elements or an external tool. Optionally, if some of the network elements are 29 located remotely either in a cloud or on the internet, the additional latency should be calculated and accounted for.  30 The Master eNB, Secondary gNB and their components (O-eNB, O-RU, O-DU and O-CU) need to have the right 31 configuration and software load. The end user device must be configured with the right user credentials to be able to 32 register and authenticate with the O-RAN system and the 4G core. The end user devices also need to be provisioned 33 with the user credentials to attach to the 4G core and register with the IMS core to perform video call using ViLTE. The 34 4G core network and IMS core must be configured to support end user devices used for testing. This includes 35 supporting registration, authentication and PDN connection establishment for these end user devices. The also includes 36 provisioning the IMS core to support registration of the end user devices to make video calls over ViLTE. The locations 37 where the radio conditions are excellent, good, fair and poor need to be identified within the serving cell. 38

In this test scenario, both the mobile originated and mobile terminated end user devices are going to use the 10 same O-RAN (i.e. same O-eNB), 4G and IMS core to perform the end-to-end video call. All traces and packet captures 11 need to be enabled for the duration of the testing to ensure all communication between network elements can be 12 captured and validated. 13 1. Power on the two end user devices in excellent radio condition and ensure both of the end user devices connect to 14 the 4G core over the Master O-eNB and optionally secondary gNB.  15 2. Use the MO end user device to video call the MT end user device. Validate the MT end user device is able to 19 receive and answer the call. 20 4. Continue to have two-way voice and video communication on the video call for at least 5 minutes before 21 terminating it.  22 5. Repeat the test multiple times (>10 times) and gather results. 23 6. Repeat the above steps 1 through 5 for the MO and MT end user devices in good, fair and poor radio conditions. 24  25 6.6.1.4 Test Expectation (expected results) 26 As a pre-validation, use the traces to validate a successful PDN connection setup by the end user devices without any 27 errors using the Master eNB and optionally the secondary gNB. Use the packet 32 captures to validate there is no RTP packet drops or high RTP packet jitter which could cause voice muting, video 33 freezing or video lag issues. This test scenario is applicable for a 5G SA deployment. 9 6.6.3.1 Test Description 10 Voice service is one of the basic services provided on the telecommunication network. Video service on the 4G network 11 was provided using ViLTE. Similarly, video service on the 5G network is provided using packet switch technology 12 called Video over New Radio. As 5G network is being deployed by a telecommunication service provider, the service 13 provider will need to support 4G and 5G network, and thus support Video over LTE, Video over NR and the handover 14 between the two video calling services. This scenario tests the end user’s video calling experience when the end user 15 device performs a handover from video over LTE to Video over NR and vice versa. 16 6.6.3.2 Test Setup 17 The SUT in this test case would be an O-eNB along with a gNB (O-RU, O-DU and O-CU). We would also need an 18 interworking 4G-5G core (referred to as 4G-5G core going forward) which supports a combined anchor point for 4G 19 and 5G, i.e. SMF+PGW-C and UPF+PGW-U. The eNB connects to a 4G-5G core over the 4G interfaces like S1 to 20 provide 4G LTE service and the O-CU, O-DU and O-RU would connect to a 4G-5G core over the 5G interfaces like N2 21 and N3 to provide 5G SA service. The O-eNB, gNB and the components within these have to comply with the O-RAN 22 specifications. The 4G-5G core will be required to support the basic functionality to authenticate and register an end 23 user device in order to setup a PDN connection/PDU session. An IMS core will be required to register the end user 24 device to support video calling services on a 4G and 5G network. The 4G and 5G core have to interwork using the N26 25 interface between the MME and AMF to support seamless handover. We are recommending the use of N26 interface to 26 ensure better customer experience when the end user device performs a handover between 4G and 5G. The 4G-5G and 27 IMS cores could be a completely emulated, partially emulated or real non-emulated cores. We will need at least two end 28 user devices (UE) which can be a real UEs or an emulated one, and both have to support video calling service using 29 ViLTE, Video over NR and the capability to handover from ViLTE to Video over NR and vice versa. The end user 30 devices will serve as Mobile Originated (MO) and Mobile Terminated (MT) end user devices forming the two ends of 31 the video call. Going forward in this section, these end user devices will be referred to as MO end user device and MT 32 end user devices to represent their role in the video call. The test setup should include tools which have the ability to 33 collect traces on the elements and/or packet captures of communication between the elements. This could be a built-in 34 capability of the emulated/non-emulated network elements or an external tool. Optionally, if some of the network 35 elements are located remotely either in a cloud or on the internet, the additional latency should be calculated and 36 accounted for. 37 The O-eNB, gNB and their components (O-RU, O-DU and O-CU) need to have the right configuration and software 38 load. Upgrade of telecom network occurs in phases, and this is no different for 5G. The telecom network 7 may not be able to support video calling service on 5G during this phase for multiple reasons – the 5G network may not 8 be deployed nationwide, or 5G network may not be tuned to support voice/video service or the devices may not be able 9 to support video/voice service on 5G. EPS fallback is the method used to support voice/video services during this 10 transition phase, where voice/video services are continued to be supported on the legacy LTE network using video over 11 LTE (ViLTE). This section of the document only applies to the video calling service provided by the telecom service 12 provider, which uses the telecom operator’s IMS core to establish dedicated bearer to provide superior video calling 13 experience. This needs to be measured on 22 both ends of the video call – mobile originated and mobile terminated and counted only once per call. 23

6.7.1 Video Service – EPS Fallback testing 1 This scenario tests the video calling service when an end user device (UE) is in 5G SA coverage and performs EPS 2 fallback to 4G to make or receive a video call. 3 6.7.1.1 Test Description 4 This section tests the video service on a 5G SA network when it uses EPS fallback mechanism to fallback to the LTE 5 network to use video over LTE to support video calling service. This testing only applies to 5G SA deployment and in 6 this scenario the UE is connected to the 5G SA Core and registered with the IMS core for video calling service. As 7 video calling service is not supported on 5G yet, the UE is forced to fallback to 4G when it makes/receives a video call. 8 The EPS fallback does increase the Call Setup Time due to the time needed to fallback before making/receiving the 9 video call.  10 There are two mechanism in which EPS fallback is supported on the 5G core, and the methodology used impacts the 11 time taken to perform the fallback to LTE, and hence impacts the Call Setup Time. The two mechanisms that could be 12 used to perform EPS fallback is included below. These two mechanisms do change the test setup but does not change 13 the testing procedure. 14 • With N26 interface – The AMF in the 5G core communicates with the MME in the 4G over the N26 interface. 15 In this scenario the UE performs a handover from the 5G network to the 4G network. 16 • Without N26 interface – The AMF in the 5G core does not communicate directly with the 4G core, instead 17 uses the UDM/HSS to store and transfer relevant session information to the 4G core. In this scenario the UE 18 performs a Release with Redirect from the 5G network to the 4G network. 19  20 6.7.1.2 Test Setup 21 The SUT in this test case would be an O-eNB along with a gNB (O-RU, O-DU and O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP). We would 22 also need an interworking 4G-5G core (referred to as 4G-5G core going forward) which supports a combined anchor 23 point, i.e. SMF+PGW-C and UPF+PGW-U. The O-eNB connects to a 4G-5G core over the 4G interfaces like S1 to 24 provide 4G LTE service and the gNB (O-RU, O-DU and O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP) would connect to the 4G-5G core over 25 the 5G interfaces like N2 and N3 to provide 5G SA service. The O-eNB, gNB and the components within these have to 26 comply with the O-RAN specifications and support EPS fallback. The 4G-5G core will be required to support the basic 27 functionality to authenticate and register an end user device in order to setup a PDN connection/PDU session. The IMS 28 core will be required to register the end user device and needs to be integrated with the 4G-5G core to support video 29 calling services over LTE (ViLTE) and EPS fallback. The 4G-5G core has to interwork either using the N26 interface 30 or without using N26 interface depending on the desired core network configuration. The existence of the N26 interface 31 does reduce the EPS fallback time, hence reduce Call Setup Time and provide better end user experience. The 4G-5G 32 and IMS cores could be a completely emulated, partially emulated or real non-emulated cores. We will need at least two 33 end user devices (UE) which can be a real UEs or an emulated one, and both have to support video calling service using 34 ViLTE and EPS fallback procedure. The end user devices will serve as Mobile Originated (MO) and Mobile 35 Terminated (MT) end user devices forming the two ends of the video call. For the sake of clarity, we will address these 36 end user devices as UE-1 and UE-2 in this section. The test setup should include tools which have the ability to collect 37 traces on the elements and/or packet captures of communication between the elements. This could be a built-in 38 capability of the emulated/non-emulated network elements or an external tool. Optionally, if some of the network 39 elements are located remotely either in a cloud or on the internet, the additional latency should be calculated and 40 accounted for.  41 The O-eNB, gNB and their components (O-RU, O-DU and O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP) need to have the right configuration 42 and software load. The end user device must be configured with the right user credentials to be able to register and 43
