The end user devices also need to be provisioned with the 1 user credentials to attach to the 4G-5G core and register with the IMS core to perform video call using ViLTE and EPS 2 fallback. The 4G-5G core network and IMS core must be configured to support video calling service on the end user 3 device used for testing, which includes dynamically setting up dedicated bearers for voice calls.  4 All the elements in the network like O-RAN system, 4G-5G core and the IMS Core need to have the ability to capture 5 traces to validate the successful execution of the test cases. The end user devices need to have the capability to capture 6 traces/packet capture to calculate the ViLTE KPIs. Optionally, the network could have network taps deployed in 7 various legs of the network to get packet captures to validate successful execution of the test cases. Finally, all these 8 different components need to have connectivity with each other – the end user device should be able to connect to O-9 RAN system(O-eNBs and gNBs), O-RAN system needs to be connected to the 4G-5G core which in turn should have 10 connectivity to the IMS Core. 11 6.7.1.3 Test Methodology/Procedure 12 Ensure the end user devices, O-RAN system, 4G-5G core and the IMS Core have all been configured as outlined in 13 Section 6.7.1.2. In this test scenario, one of the end user devices (UE-1) is going to be connected over the 4G O-eNB to 14 the 4G-5G core, while the other end user device (UE-2) is going to be connected over 5G gNB (O-RU, O-DU and O-15 CU-CP/O-CU-UP) to the 4G-5G core. All traces and packet captures need to be enabled for the duration of the testing 16 to ensure all communication between network elements can be captured and validated. 17 1. Power on the two end user devices and ensure UE-1 is in LTE coverage, when UE-2 is in the 5G coverage. 18 Validate both end user devices register to the 4G-5G core. 19 2. Once the registration is complete, UE-1 and UE-2 have to establish a PDN connection and PDU session 20 respectively with the 4G-5G core. Once the PDN connection and PDU session have been setup, both the end user 21 devices have to register with the IMS core to support video calling services. 22 3. Use UE-1 as the MO end user device to video call UE-2. Validate the UE-2 performs EPS fallback to LTE to 23 receive the video call.  24 4. Answer the call on UE-2 and continue to have two-way audio/video communication for at least 5 minutes and 25 terminate the call. Measure the voice KPIs included in Section 6.7.  26 5. At this point UE-2 should have completed the video call and moved back to connect to the 4G-5G Core over 5G 27 gNB (i.e. O-RU). 28 6. Use UE-2 as the MO end user device to call UE-1. Validate the UE-2 performs EPS fallback to LTE to before 29 making the video call.  30 7. Answer the call on UE-1 and continue to have two-way communication for at least 5 minutes and terminate the 31 call. Measure the voice KPIs included in Section 6.7.  32 8. Repeat the test multiple times (>10 times) and gather results. 33  34 6.7.1.4 Test Expectation (expected results) 35 As a pre-validation, use the traces to validate end user device UE-1 is able to perform a successful registration and PDN 36 connection setup while using the O-eNB in LTE coverage. Similarly, use the traces to validate end user device UE-2 is 37 able to perform a successful registration and PDU session setup while using gNB in 5G coverage. Also validate both the 38 end user devices are able to register with the IMS core for voice services. This is a prerequisite before these tests can be 39 validated. 40 Validate the end user devices are able to make a video call between each other by dynamically setting up a QCI-2 41 bearer to transfer voice and video packets over RTP. Validate the device which is in the 5G coverage falls back to 4G 42 before receiving or making a video call, in other words the EPS fallback procedure has been executed successfully. The 43 Call Setup Time will be higher than Video over LTE due to the delay associated with executing the EPS fallback 44 procedure. Even though this test case does not directly impact the quality of the video call, for the sake of consistency, 45

The details of these features, along with any other features/functionality which 2 could impact the end user’s video calling experience should be included in the test report to provide a comprehensive 3 view of the testing. 4 6.8 Video Service – Video over NR 5 Video calling service is slowly replacing voice as a basic calling service. The improvement in end user throughput on 6 LTE has facilitated the move from voice call to video, and the high speeds on 5G is only going to accelerate this 7 migration process. This section of the document validates the user’s video calling experience in different conditions on 8 the 5G network. This section of the document only applies to the video calling service provided by the telecom service 9 provider, which uses the telecom operator’s IMS core to establish dedicated QoS flows to provide superior video calling 10 experience. The KPIs which will be monitored to assess the video calling service are included below 11 • CSSR – Call Setup Success Rate % – Total number of calls which were successful by the total number of 12 calls made as a percentage. 13 • CST – Call Setup Time – Time taken from the initial SIP INVITE to when the SIP 180 Ringing is received 14 in seconds. 15 • MOS Score – Mean Opinion Score for the video call. This needs to be measured on both ends of the video 16 call – mobile originated and mobile terminated. 17 • Mute Rate % – This is the percentage of video calls which were muted or video freezes in both directions 18 (calls with RTP loss of > 3-4s in both directions are considered muted call). This needs to be measured on 19 both ends of the video call – mobile originated and mobile terminated and counted only once per call. 20 • One Way Calls % – This is the percentage of video calls which were muted, or video is not transmitted in 21 any one direction only (video calls with RTP loss of > 3-4s in one direction only are considered one-way 22 calls). This needs to be monitored on both ends of the Voice call – mobile originated and mobile terminated 23 and counted only once per call. 24 • RTP Packet Loss % - Number of RTP packets which were dropped/lost in uplink/downlink direction as a 25 percentage of total packets. This needs to be measured on both ends of the Voice call – mobile originated 26 and mobile terminated. 27

Continue the two-way video and voice communication between MO and MT end user devices until the handover 39 procedure is complete before terminating the video call.  40 6. Repeat the test (steps 1 through 5) multiple times (> 10 times) and gather results. 41  42

The details of these features, along with any other features/functionality 2 which could impact the end user’s video calling service experience should be included in the test report to provide a 3 comprehensive view of the testing. 4 6.9 URLLC 5 The 5G network has been built not just to support the data and voice use-cases of the past, but a wide variety of use-6 cases to support connectivity across a diverse set of verticals like industrial automation, health care, gaming etc. 5G 7 network was built with flexibility and efficiency with additional enhancements being added in every release to enable 8 ultra-low latency and/or ultra-reliability to cater to a diverse set of use-cases such as automotive applications, augment 9 and virtual reality etc. Network Slicing in 5G allows the separation of the network where services with different 10 characteristics and requirements can be run on its own slice of the network. These enhancements in 5G paired up with 11 network slicing capability allows a telecommunication service provider to support all the diverse set of use-cases using 12 the same 5G network on different network slices.   13 Ultra-Reliable Low Latency Communication (URLLC) is one such slice which allows service which require low E2E 14 latency to be deployed. As this is a new concept, new services and use-cases are being created to use this slice of the 15 network. The generic KPIs which will be monitored to assess the URLLC use cases are included below. As the URLLC 16 use cases vary, the KPI used and the values for these KPIs also vary by use case. Not all KPIs are used for all use cases 17 and similarly some KPIs are used only in specific use cases. 18 • Reliability – This is the percentage of packets or messages sent which were successfully delivered to the 19 end node. 20 • End to End Latency – Time taken to transfer a packet/message from the end user device to the application 21 or vice versa. 22 • Jitter – This is the variation of the end-to-end latency values seen in the network setup. 23 • Position Service Accuracy – This is the distance between the location provided by the location service and 24 the real location of the target object.  25 • Position Service Latency – Time elapsed between the request to get location information to when the 26 location information is available. 27 • User experienced throughput – The throughput as measured on the application layer.  28 • Mean time between failures – This is the duration of time the service was available before a failure 29 condition which causes the service to be unavailable. 30  31 This section of the document gives the procedure to test the URLLC applications in a telecommunication network. 32 URLLC refers to a diverse set of applications where the end-user device communicates to the application server over 33 the 5G network. The use-case itself has stringent requirements for the end-to-end latency and/or reliability between the 34 end-user device and application server. 5G has enhancements to its RAN and core to support these requirements to 35 support these use cases. The 5G RAN allows for lower downlink data to uplink Ack, thus reducing the latency on the air 36 interface. Similarly, 5G Core allows for the data plane anchor (UPF) to be deployed close to the end user device in the 37 edge location along with the application server to eliminate part of the backhaul latency.   38

O-RAN.TIFG.E2E-Test.0-v04.00 O-RAN.TIFG.E2E-Test.0-v04.00 Please see below summary table of test cases and applicable technology.  1 Table 6-32: Augmented Reality Test Case summary 2   Applicable technology  Test case Functional group LTE 
NSA 
SA Test ID URLLC-Augmented Reality          6.9.1 Augmented Reality  Service N/A N/A Y  3  4 6.9.1 Augmented Reality 5 This test scenario validates the end user’s augmented reality experience when using an AR device over the 5G network. 6 6.9.1.1 Test Description 7 This section of the document is using Augmented Reality (AR) as an example, where the end user device is an AR 8 device which communicates with an image processing server which acts as the application server. AR itself has a wide 9 range of applications including gaming, augmented worker as a part of smart factory, remote maintenance, ad-hoc 10 support from remote expert and control of heavy equipment etc. AR is one such use case where AR device has a camera 11 and continuously transmits images in real time to an application server, along with the position of the AR device. The 12 application server in this case would be an image processing server, which render the augmented image and transmit 13 down the augmented video stream back to the AR device, which is then displayed on the AR device. Here the entire 14 process of uploading the image from the AR device to the processing on the application server, and the displaying of the 15 augmented image on the AR device has to occur within milliseconds with high reliability to ensure the end user has a 16 realistic augmented reality experience.  17 The 5G URLLC network slice does not have requirements on the protocol, or the method used to communicate between 18 the end user device and the application server. The 5G core acts as a pipe to allow communication between these two 19 end points, AR device and application server, with high data rate, reliability and latency requirements as dictated by the 20 application. Optionally, depending on the AR use case two additional KPIs could also be monitored when audio-visual 21 interaction is characterized by a human being interacting with entities or humans by relying on audio-visual feedback. 22 • Motion to photon – This is the latency between the physical movement of the user’s head and the updated 23 image on the AR device. 24 • Motion to audio – This is the latency between the physical movement of the user’s head and the updated sound 25 waves from the AR device’s speakers. 26  27 6.9.1.2 Test Setup 28 The SUT in this test case would be a gNB (O-RU, O-DU and O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP) which is used to test the URLLC 29 use case. A 5G SA Core would be required to support basic functionality to authenticate and register the end user device 30 to establish a PDU session. An image processing server would act as an application server for this test scenario. The 5G 31 core and application server could be a completely emulated, partially emulated or real non-emulated core and image 32 processing server. For the testing of this use case, we could use a 35 real end user device or an emulated one. The test setup should include tools which have the ability to collect traces on 36

All traces and packet captures need to be enabled for the duration of the testing to ensure all 22 communication between network elements can be captured and validated. 23 1. Power on the end user device in excellent radio condition and ensure the device registers with the 5G core over SA 24 by connecting over the O-RAN (O-RU, O-DU and O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP). 25 2. Once the registration is complete, the end user device has to establish a PDU session over the URLLC network 26 slice, followed by connection to the application server. 27 3. Use the end user device to communicate with the application server for an AR session. Depending on the end user 28 device, this could be done using a finger and/or hand to select the right AR application, or using a button or touch 29 activated system to select the right AR application.   30 4. Once the application has started, complete the designated task which could be playing a game or performing a pre-31 defined task on a machinery, troubleshooting etc. 32 5. Repeat the test multiple times (> 10 times) and gather results. 33 6. Repeat the above steps 1 through 5 for the good, fair and poor radio conditions. 34  35 6.9.1.4 Test Expectation (expected results) 36 As a pre-validation, use the traces to validate a successful registration and PDU session setup to the URLLC network 37 slice by the end user device without any errors over O-RU, O-DU and O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP. Also validate the end user 38 device is able to connect to the application server for the AR session. This is a prerequisite before these tests can be 39 validated. 40 Validate the end user device is able to setup the AR session and perform the desired task eg: Remote maintenance. 41 Ensure there is no noticeable video freezing, jitter or lag in the AR session for the end user. Use packet captures to 42 validate there is no jitter or packet drops between the end user device and the application server. Use the packet captures 43 to ensure there are no out-of-sequence packets which could impact the end user’s experience. Calculate the metrics like 44 latency and throughput using the packet captures. 45

6.10 mMTC 7 The 5G network also supports a wide set of use cases which allow machines type devices to communicate with network 8 to realise the Internet of Things use cases. These use cases fall under a category called mMTC – massive Machine Type 9 Communication. There are a diverse set of use cases under this category which supports a range of verticals including 10 security, healthcare, remote control, metering, smart city to name of few. The 5G network has enhanced the RAN and 11 the core to support mMTC requirements. Some of these enhancements include support large number of mMTC devices 12 

  
_______________________________________________________________________________________________.                © 2022 by the O-RAN ALLIANCE e.V. Your use is subject to the copyright statement on the cover page of this specification. 179  
O-RAN.TIFG.E2E-Test.0-v04.00 O-RAN.TIFG.E2E-Test.0-v04.00 
by improving coverage and density of devices. Enhancements also include reducing the overhead for small intermittent 1 data transmission, thus ensuring low energy usage, and extending the battery life of the mMTC devices. 2 Network slicing in 5G allows for a telecommunication service provider to support these diverse set of mMTC use cases 3 by dedicating a separate slice of the network for these use cases. 3GPP has also defined a massive Machine Type 4 Communication (mMTC) as a slice which is used to support the use case to support very large number of small devices 5 (million to billions) in an efficient way so as to ensure optimal energy utilization. This section of the document includes 6 scenarios to test service(s) which use the mMTC slice. 7 Please see below summary table of test cases and applicable technology.  8 Table 6-35: mMTC-Sensors Test Case Selection summary 9   Applicable technology  Test case Functional group LTE 
NSA 
SA Test ID mMTC-Sensors          6.10.1 Augmented Reality  Service N/A N/A Y  10  11 6.10.1 Sensors 12 This test scenario validates the working of sensors over an mMTC slice on a 5G network. 13 6.10.1.1 Test Description 14 This section of the document gives the procedure to test the mMTC applications in a telecommunication network. As 15 already mentioned, mMTC comprises of a diverse set of use cases supported across a various vertical. Most of the 16 mMTC use cases include an end user equipment which communicates with an application server over the 5G network. 17 The end user device varies based on the use case from a sensor which measure temperature or humidity or weight etc to 18 a gauge which measures usage of electricity or water etc to a monitoring device which monitor vital statistics. These 19 end user devices collect data and update the application server periodically. The application server collects the data, 20 stores it, analyses it, and can also perform additional tasks if needed based on the outcome of the analysis eg: turn off 21 equipment when the temperature is high etc.  22 This section of the document is using sensors to perform mMTC testing. The test case includes deploying 5G sensors in 23 the field to collect data and upload it to an application server. The sensors itself could vary based on the use case needed 24 to support. These sensors could be air humidity sensor, temperature sensor and soil moisture sensor used in conjunction 25 to identify the need to irrigate a farm. These sensors could also be used to identify gas leaks to alert the authorities or 26 turn off gas lines. These sensors could also be light/image sensors which can be used to identify if a parking spot is 27 occupied. There are numerous sensors which support a varied set of use cases, but this test case has been intentionally 28 left generic without specifying the type of sensor that will be used for testing. 29 The 5G mMTC network slice does not have requirements on the protocol, or the method used to communicate between 30 the end user device and the application server. The 5G core acts as a pipe to allow communication between these two 31 end points, the sensors and application server, with the low energy utilization and the ability to support massive number 32 of such sensors to connect. 33

 Security 1 This chapter describes the tests evaluating and assessing the security aspects of the E2E of a radio access network. The 2 general test methodologies and configurations outlined in Chapter 3 should be followed.  3 Because the whole O-RAN system is the System under Test (SUT) and can be viewed as an integrated black box in the 4 context of the E2E testing, the security test cases listed here fall into the following categories: 5 1. gNB security assurance specification required by 3GPP SA3 (applicable to 5G-NR NSA/SA); 6 2. Optional: Denial of Service (DoS), fuzzing, and exploitation types of security test cases against the O-RAN 7 system executed on L2 (Ethernet) and L3 (IP) layers with easy to access target information (MAC/IP address) 8 (applicable to 5G-NR NSA/SA); 9 3. Optional: Resource exhaustion types of security test case against the underlying cloud infrastructure hosting 10 the O-RAN system and its network slice(s) (applicable to 5G-NR NSA/SA)   11 Any other security test cases requiring specific access to a major interface or an internal functionality of the O-RAN 12 component(s) is out of scope. 13 7.1 gNB Security Assurance Specification (SCAS) required by 14 3GPP SA3 15 An E2E O-RAN system (SUT) is equivalent to a gNB from both network architecture and functional perspectives, 16 therefore any gNB specific security requirements, threats and test cases outlined in 3GPP TS 33.511 [27] shall be 17 followed in this E2E of radio access network security evaluation and assessment. O-RAN.TIFG.E2E-Test.0-v04.00 O-RAN.TIFG.E2E-Test.0-v04.00  1 7.2 Optional: DoS, fuzzing and blind exploitation types of security 2 test 3 Due to the open and disaggregated nature of the O-RAN system (SUT), the attack surfaces associated with some of its 4 critical transport protocols and major interfaces of the O-RAN system become easy targets for potential attackers. 5 Cyberattacks like DoS, fuzzing and blind exploitation types are easy to launch, require little information on the target 6 system, and could cause significant performance degradation, or even the service interruption if not properly mitigated. 7 As the WG1 Security Task Group (STG) is continuously working on the O-RAN system security analysis and security 8 requirements specifications, the proposed security test cases in this section are optional for this E2E test specification 9 release.  10 As we learn more about the O-RAN system security posture against these types of attacks through the proposed test 11 cases, we will reevaluate their necessity in the subsequent E2E test specification releases. 12 The duration of test TRAFFIC GENERATION specified in this section should be at least 3 minutes. 13 Please see below summary table of test cases and applicable technology.  14 Table 7-2: DoS and Fuzzing Test Case Selection summary 15   Applicable technology  Test case Functional group

Among various types of threats to the O-Cloud infrastructure, resource exhaustion attack, such as the O-Cloud side-1 channel noisy neighbor attack, is one of the easiest to launch and could impact the network slice(s) performance, 2 especially on a small size edge cloud with shared physical hosts/resources. 3 As the WG1 Security Task Group (STG) is continuously working on the O-RAN system security analysis and security 4 requirements specifications, the proposed security test case in this section will be optional for this E2E test specification 5 release.  6 As we learn more about the O-RAN system security posture against these types of attacks through the proposed test 7 case, we will re-evaluate its necessity in the following E2E test specification release. 8 Please see below summary table of test cases and applicable technology.  9 Table 7-3: O-Cloud resource exhaustion Test Case Selection summary 10   Applicable technology  Test case Functional group

 Load and Stress Tests 1 This chapter describes the tests evaluating and assessing the load and stress tests of the radio access network from a 2 network end-to-end perspective. The focus of the testing is on the tolerability of the eNB/gNB under load based on 3 3GPP and O-RAN specifications. 4  5 The eNB/gNB should have an ability to process the various traffic patterns which could happen in a real field. Load and 6 stress tests are used to evaluate the tolerability of eNB/gNB against load. Load and stress tests are a test methodology to 7 be performed in the laboratory to simulate the actual traffic load that eNB/gNB are subjected to in a real field. Load and 8 stress tests help to detect performance-related problems of the entire system, which are "less reproducible" and difficult 9 to solve in general. In some cases, even problems that could not be found by functional testing are discovered. Load and 10 stress tests in addition to Functional testing will help to ensure the final quality of the eNB/gNB products. It will lead to 11 improve the overall quality of the system, which in turn will improve the user experience. 12  13 Please see below summary table of test cases and applicable technology.  14 Table 8-1: Load and Stress Tests summary 15   Applicable technology  Test case Functional group LTE 
NSA 
SA TEST ID Load and Stress Tests Load and Stress       8.1 Simultaneous RRC_CONNECTED UEs Load and Stress Y Y Y 8.2 Benchmark of UE State Transition Load and Stress Y Y Y 8.3 Traffic Load Testing Load and Stress Y N/A Y 8.4 Traffic Model Testing Load and Stress Y Y Y 8.5 Long hours stability Testing Load and Stress Y Y Y 8.6 Multi-cell Testing Load and Stress Y Y Y 8.7 Emergency call Load and Stress Y Y Y 8.8 ETWS (Earthquake and Tsunami Warning System) Load and Stress Y Y Y  16 
8.1 Simultaneous RRC_CONNECTED UEs 17 
8.1.1 Test description and applicability 18 The purpose of this test is to connect multiple UEs to the eNB/gNB and measure the maximum number of UEs that can 19 be simultaneously maintained in RRC_CONNECTED state. By connecting multiple UEs at the same time, a basic 20 eNB/gNB capacity is verified. This test is valid with either LTE or 5G NSA/SA. The test procedure is to stack the 21 number of connected UEs to the eNB/gNB one by one and check the maximum number of simultaneous connected 22 UEs. To achieve this test, the connected UE needs to transmit a minimum size of U-Plane packet periodically such as 23 ping to keep the RRC_CONNECTED state. The transmit interval of the packets should be shorter than RRC inactivity 24 timer value in eNB/gNB. Figure 8-1 Simultaneous RRC_CONNECTED UEsillustrates how the test works.  25  26

8.1.3 Test Procedure 21 The test steps below are applicable for either LTE or 5G NSA/SA: 22 1. The test setup is configured according to the test configuration. The test configuration should be recorded in the test 23 report. The serving cell under test is activated and unloaded. All other cells are powered off. 24 2. The UEs (emulated UE) are placed under excellent radio conditions (Cell centre close to radiated eNB/gNB 25 Antenna) as defined by LTE RSRP (for LTE) or 5G SS-RSRP (for 5G NSA/SA) in Section 3.6.  26 3. The End-to-end setup shall be operational for LTE or 5G NSA/SA as applicable for the test scenario, and there 27 should not be any connectivity issues.  28 4. Required performance data (incl. the signalling and control data) as specified in the “Test requirements” Section 29 below is measured and captured at the UE(s) and eNB/gNB side using logging/measurement tools. 30

5. "Power ON" the UEs one by one, connect them to the LTE or 5G NSA/SA cell, and confirm that they are in the 1 RRC_CONNECTED state normally. The “Power ON” intervals may be better to longer than the attach latency 2 described in Chapter4. Increase the number of UEs until the newly powered UE fails to connect to LTE or 5G 3 NSA/SA cell. The UE in  RRC_CONNECTED state, to keep their RRC connection, should send some data packets 4 like Ping. At this time, it is necessary to set the connection holding time at least 3 minutes in Figure 8 -1 to be 5 sufficiently long. 6 6. Check the measured RRC Connected UEs for the SUT and if the measured value is not consistent with expectations 7 for the entire SUT (O-DU/O-CU) being fully loaded, use the optional OFH and/or F1 interface and test equipment 8 (see Section 3.1.1) to add more UEs maintaining RRC_CONNECTED state until the entire SUT is fully utilized 9 (e.g. >80%) during execution of the test case procedure. After the holding time, check the maximum number of 10 UEs that were in the state of RRC_CONNECTED simultaneously. 11 7. Lost connections shall be re-established automatically to maximize number of RRC Connected UEs. 12 8. Stop and save the test logs. Check the log to make sure that the test runs successfully and that no unexpected 13 behavior such as unexpected call release is recorded. The logs should be captured and kept for test result reference 14 and measurements. 15

Check the maximum number of UE connections. 8.2 Benchmark of UE State Transition 27 
8.2.1 Test description and applicability 28  29 The purpose of this test is to verify the benchmark value of the number of UE state transitions that can be processed per 30 unit time by connecting multiple UEs to an eNB/gNB. 31 As shown in Figure 8-2, a certain number of UEs (e.g. 100 UEs, 200 UEs … etc. Attach them at reasonable stack speed 32 one by one, 10 UEs per second for example.) repeatedly performs UE state transitions (transition RRC_IDLE to/from 33 RRC_CONNECTED) for a certain period of time (X seconds). The number of UE calls is increased by a fixed number 34 (e.g. 100 UEs). If a finer resolution is required, it is increased by 10 UEs. The number of UE calls in the X seconds 35 interval is incrementally increased, and the test is performed until a fail to connect occurs as shown in the example of 36

O-RAN.TIFG.E2E-Test.0-v04.00 O-RAN.TIFG.E2E-Test.0-v04.00 
the right end of Figure 8-2. By connecting multiple UEs at the same time, the basic eNB/gNB control plane processing 1 capability is verified. This test is valid with either LTE or 5G NSA/SA. 2 The test procedure is to gradually increase the number of UEs connected to the eNB/gNB and check the maximum 3 number of simultaneously connected UEs. In order to transition the RRC_CONNECTED state and the RRC_IDLE state 4 of the UE alternately and continuously, the UE needs to transmit a minimum size of U-Plane packet periodically such as 5 ping. The transmit interval of the packets should be longer than RRC inactivity timer value in eNB/gNB. By repeating 6 state transitions by many UEs, test a limit of eNB/gNB processing capacity. In addition, by repeating X seconds the call 7 generation and call release, confirm that there are no problems such as processing delays and memory release leaks 8 shall be confirmed. 9  10

8.2.3 Test Procedure 1 The test steps below are applicable for either LTE or 5G NSA/SA: 2 1. The test setup is configured according to the test configuration. The test configuration should be recorded in the test 3 report. The serving cell under test is activated and unloaded. All other cells are powered off. 4 2. The UEs (emulated UE) are placed under excellent radio conditions (Cell centre close to radiated eNB/gNB 5 Antenna) as defined by LTE RSRP (for LTE) or 5G SS-RSRP (for 5G NSA/SA) in Section 3.6.  6 3. The End-to-end setup shall be operational for LTE or 5G NSA/SA as applicable for the test scenario, and there 7 should not be any connectivity issues.  8 4. Required performance data (incl. the signalling and control data) as specified in the “Test requirements” Section 9 below is measured and captured at the UE(s) and eNB/gNB side using logging/measurement tools.  10 5. “Power ON” the UEs (emulated UE) to attach to the LTE or 5G NSA/SA cell one by one at reasonable stack speed, 11 10 UEs per second for example. Wait for a successful attach. Each UEs state transition can be checked by the state 12 of receiving control plane messages. 13 6. Ensure that all UEs perform state transitions at regular intervals for X seconds (at least 3minutes) and verify that 14 they are processed correctly. All UEs are then "Power off". It should be checked whether there are any UEs that 15 have failed to make the RRC state transition at the eNB/gNB. 16 7. Check the number of UEs that can be processed per unit time. Increase the number of UEs and repeat step 5-6 in 17 the above until processes such as state transition is no longer working properly to identify the upper limit of 18 processing capacity. The number of UE calls is increased by a fixed number (100 UEs). If a finer resolution is 19 required, it is increased by 10 UEs. 20 8. Check the maximum measured RRC Connected UEs for the SUT in step 5-7 and if the measured value is not 21 consistent with expectations for the entire SUT (O-DU/O-CU) being fully loaded, use the optional OFH and/or F1 22 interface and test equipment (see Section 3.1.1) to add more UEs and RRC Connected state transitions until the 23 entire SUT is fully loaded (e.g. >80%) during execution of the test case procedure. 24 9. Stop and save the test logs. Check the log to make sure that the test runs successfully and that no unexpected 25 behavior such as unexpected call release is recorded. The logs should be captured and kept for test result reference 26 and measurements.  27

Check the rate of UE state transition that can be processed per 34 unit time. 8.3 Traffic Load Testing 1 
8.3.1 Test description and applicability 2 The purpose of this test is to check the stability of the eNB/gNB under load, with a large number of UEs sending and 3 receiving user data. By connecting a large number of UEs at the same time, the eNB/gNB processing capacity and 4 stability are verified. The maximum cell throughput is discussed in Chapter 5 and is not a scope of this Chapter. This 5 test is valid with either LTE or 5G SA. 6 The test procedure is to connect X UEs (e.g. 10UEs) per second to the eNB/gNB with UDP download/upload traffic 7 until reach N UEs (e.g. 1,000UEs) connected. Then disconnect X UEs per second and connected X new UEs to the 8 eNB/gNB with UDP download/upload traffic. It is to continue disconnecting and connecting UEs near the maximum 9 number of UEs to verify a stability of eNB/gNB.  It is recommended that the total UDP traffic be less than the 10 maximum cell throughput, as maximum cell throughput is out of scope for this test. 11 In this test, the number of UEs is assumed to be large, which can reach 1000. For the values of X and N, the test results 12 in the previous Section 8.1 and 8.2 may be helpful. 13 8.3.2 Test setup and configuration 14 The test setup is a single cell scenario (i.e. isolated cell without any inter-cell interference – see Section 3.7) with UEs 15 (emulated) placed under excellent radio conditions as defined in Section 3.6, using LTE RSRP (for LTE) or 5G SS-16 RSRP (for 5G SA) as the metric.   17 Since this test scenario requires a large number of UE connections, using an equipment that emulates a large number of 18 UEs and performing the test is recommended. 19 The performance of the SUT 3GPP stack components in all of the O-RU, O-DU, and O-CU sub-nodes should be 20 measured while all sub-nodes are under high load. To achieve this it may be recommended to run an additional test 21 execution step adding additional traffic utilizing the optional OFH and/or F1 interface and test equipment as described 22 in Section 3.1.1 in addition to the traffic applied over the configured RF cell. 23 Test configuration: The test configuration is not specified. The utilized test configuration (connection diagram between 24 SUT and test equipment, parameters) should be recorded in the test report.  25 Laboratory setup: The radio conditions experienced by the UE can be modified using a variable attenuator inserted 26 between the antenna connectors (if available) of the O-RU and the UE, or appropriately emulated using a UE emulator. 27 The test environment should be setup to achieve excellent radio conditions (LTE RSRP (for LTE) or 5G SS-RSRP (for 28 5G SA) as defined in Section 3.6) for the UE, but the minimum coupling loss (see Section 3.6) should not be exceeded.  29  30 8.3.3 Test Procedure 31 The test steps below are applicable for either LTE or 5G SA: 32 1. The test setup is configured according to the test configuration. The test configuration should be recorded in the test 33 report. The serving cell under test is activated and unloaded. All other cells are powered off. 34 2. The UEs (emulated UE) are placed under excellent radio conditions (Cell centre close to radiated eNB/gNB 35 Antenna) as defined by LTE RSRP (for LTE) or 5G SS-RSRP (for 5G SA) in Section 3.6.  36 3. The End-to-end setup shall be operational for LTE or 5G SA as applicable for the test scenario, and there should 37 not be any connectivity issues.  38

4. Keep X UEs per second to access the eNB/gNB by signalling, initiate UDP upload/download for each UE after UEs 3 get access. In this step and later steps, lost connections shall be re-established automatically to maintain number of 4 RRC Connected UEs. 5 6. 8.4 Traffic Model Testing 1 
8.4.1 Test description and applicability 2 The purpose of this test is to check the performance under varying load for the eNB/gNB by generating various UE 3 behavior such as Attach/Detach, Registration/Deregistration, Data upload/download, and Mobility based on the traffic 4 model to load the eNB/gNB. Traffic models can vary depending on factors such as geographic characteristics of the 5 target area, UE type, number of UEs, UE mobility, time of day, and so on. It is important to consider the maximum 6 number of UEs and the behavior that it is expected in the field, and develop the appropriate traffic model. This test is 7 valid for either LTE or 5G NSA/SA. The test procedure is to cause various UE behavior such as Attach/Detach, 8 Registration/Deregistration, Data upload/download, and Mobility to the eNB/gNB. Repeat various scenarios based on 9 the traffic model. By processing complex combinations of scenario, the processing capacity of the eNB/gNB is 10 confirmed. Review the KPIs for each scenario to see if there are any issues that depend on a particular scenario. 11  12 
8.4.1.1 Traffic model 13 The traffic model is a model of the set of scenarios that an eNB/gNB is expected to undergo in the actual field. The test 14 items in Chapter 4 and 6 can be part of the traffic model scenario as they are or by simplifying them. Each scenario may 15 be repeated continuously at a certain frequency, or many may be executed simultaneously. The pattern of possible 16 scenarios may be differs depending on the characteristics of deployment area in which the eNB/gNB is deployed and 17 can be categorized into several typical types. It is necessary to have eNB/gNB capabilities that are appropriate for the 18 deployment area. See Table 8.4-1 for examples of typical traffic model types. 19 Table 8-6 Example of typical traffic model types 20 No. Traffic Model Type  Major Characteristics 1 Indoor Hotspot  The number of UEs may be very large, not much fluctuation in the number of UEs (less handover).  High traffic volume in data transmission and reception. 2 Dense Urban  The number of UEs is very large.  High traffic volume in data transmission and reception.  High in mobility.  Less active at night 3 Office area  The number of UEs is large.  High traffic volume in data transmission and reception.  High in mobility. Slower speed.  Less active at night 4 Residential area  The number of UEs is small.  Data traffic volume is low during daytime, may peaks at night in area where Wi-Fi is not widespread.   Low in mobility  Somehow active during daytime 5 Rural  The number of UEs is very small.  Low traffic volume in data.  Low in mobility. 6 High Speed (Train Station. Along the railroad tracks.) 
 The number of UEs may be temporarily concentrated in large numbers. Burst when a train arrives or departs, or when a train passes by.  As a train passes, many UEs pass at high speed at the same time.  21

8.5 Long hours stability Testing 3 
8.5.1 Test description and applicability 4 The purpose of this test is to conduct a load test using the traffic model described in Section 8.4.1.1 for a long period 5 (e.g. 24 to 48 hours) to confirm the continuous operation for a long period of time. This test is valid with either LTE or 6 5G NSA and SA. The test procedure is to cause various UE behavior such as Attach/Detach, 7 Registration/Deregistration, and Mobility based on the traffic model to the eNB/gNB, and maintains its performance for 8 a long time. Verify the KPIs for each scenario by repeating call generation and call release for various traffic. 9 
8.5.2 Test setup and configuration 10 The test setup is a single RF cell scenario (i.e. isolated cell without any inter-cell interference – see Section 3.7) with 11 UEs(emulated) placed under excellent radio conditions as defined in Section 3.6, using LTE RSRP (for LTE) or 5G SS-12 RSRP (for 5G NSA/SA) as the metric.   13 Since this test scenario requires a large number of UE connections, using an equipment that emulates a large number of 14 UEs and performing the test is recommended.  15 The extended length performance and stability of the SUT 3GPP stack components in all of the O-RU, O-DU, and O-16 CU sub-nodes should be measured while all sub-nodes are under high load. To achieve this it may be recommended to 17 apply additional traffic utilizing the optional OFH and/or F1 interface and test equipment as described in Section 3.1.1 18 in addition to the traffic applied at the configured RF cell. 19  20 Test configuration: The test configuration is not specified. The utilized test configuration (connection diagram between 21 SUT and test equipment, parameters) should be recorded in the test report.  22 Laboratory setup: The radio conditions experienced by the UE can be modified using a variable attenuator inserted 23 between the antenna connectors (if available) of the O-RU and the UE, or appropriately emulated using a UE emulator. 24 The test environment should be setup to achieve excellent radio conditions (LTE RSRP (for LTE) or 5G SS-RSRP (for 25 5G NSA/SA) as defined in Section 3.6) for the UE, but the minimum coupling loss (see Section 3.6) should not be 26 exceeded.  27

Check the log to make sure that the test runs successfully and that no unexpected 36 behavior such as unexpected call release is recorded. The logs should be captured and kept for test result reference 37 and measurements. 38 8. If measured RLC, RRC, and and/or PDCP KPIs are not consistent with expectations for a highly loaded O-DU and 39 O-CU, repeat steps 4 through 7 with similar incremental traffic being applied using the optional OFH and/or F1 40 interface and test equipment (see Section 3.1.1) as was used for Section 8.3.3 (Traffic Load Testing) or 8.4.3 41 (Traffic Model Testing) in addition to the modelled traffic applied at the RF interface 42

8.7.1 Test description and applicability 14 The purpose of this test is to ensure that emergency calls, such as 911/112, are preferentially established even if the 15 eNB/gNB is overloaded. This test is valid with either LTE or 5G NSA and SA. The test procedure is to further increase 16 the heavy traffic model to load the eNB/gNB, creating an overload condition where some behavior of the UE, such as 17 data upload/download, fails. Verify that emergency calls, such as 911/112, is able to communicate correctly in such a 18 situation. 19  20 The Emergency Call performance of the SUT 3GPP stack components in all of the O-RU, O-DU, and O-CU sub-nodes 21 should be measured while all sub-nodes are under high load. If additional traffic utilizing the optional OFH and/or F1 22 interface and test equipment as described in Section 3.1.1 was applied in Section 8.3.3 (Traffic Load Testing) or Section 23 8.4.3 (Traffic Model Testing) in addition to the traffic applied at the configured RF cell, the same additional traffic 24 should be applied for the Emergency Call test case. 25  26

8.7.3 Test Procedure 6 The test steps below are applicable for either LTE or 5G NSA and SA: 7 1. The test setup is configured according to the test configuration. The test configuration should be recorded in the test 8 report. The serving cell under test is activated and unloaded. All other cells are powered off. 9 2. The UE (real or emulated UE) is placed under excellent radio conditions (Cell centre close to radiated eNB/gNB 10 Antenna) as defined by LTE RSRP (for LTE) or 5G SS-RSRP (for 5G NSA/SA) in Section 3.6.  11 3. The End-to-end setup shall be operational for LTE or 5G NSA/SA as applicable for the test scenario, and there 12 should not be any connectivity issues.  13 4. Required performance data (incl. the signalling and control data) as specified in the “Test requirements” Section 14 below is measured and captured at the UE(s) and eNB/gNB side using logging/measurement tools. 15 5. If during execution of Section 8.3.3 (Traffic Load Testing) or 8.4.3 (Traffic Model Testing) incremental traffic 16 was applied using the optional OFH and/or F1 interface and test equipment (see Section 3.1.1) in addition to the 17 traffic being applied at the RF interface, the same level of traffic should be applied again using the optional OFH 18 and/or F1 interface and test equipment to ensure O-DU and O-CU exposed to adequate “base load” before 19 executing Step 6.  20 6. Start the operation of each UE according to the traffic model. 21 7. Review the KPIs for each scenario in the traffic model to ensure that the KPIs are degraded due to overload. If there 22 is no degradation of the KPI, increase the traffic model until there is degradation of the KPI (until it becomes 23 overloaded). 24 8. Make multiple emergency calls (100 times suggested) such as 911/112 to confirm if the call can be established. 25 9. Stop and save the test logs. Check the log to make sure that the test runs successfully and that no unexpected 26 behavior such as unexpected call release is recorded. The logs should be captured and kept for test result reference 27 and measurements. 28

8.8 ETWS (Earthquake and Tsunami Warning System) 1 
8.8.1 Test description and applicability 2 The purpose of this test is to verify that the ETWS notification is successful even if the eNB/gNB is overloaded. This 3 test is valid with either LTE or 5G NSA and SA. The test procedure is to further increase the heavy traffic model to load 4 the eNB/gNB, creating an overload condition where some behavior of the UE, such as data upload/download, fails. 5 Verify that the ETWS Primary and Secondary notifications can communicate correctly under these conditions. 6 The ETWS performance of the SUT 3GPP stack components in all of the O-RU, O-DU, and O-CU sub-nodes should be 7 measured while all sub-nodes are under high load. If additional traffic utilizing the optional OFH and/or F1 interface 8 and test equipment as described in Section 3.1.1 was applied in Section 8.3.3 (Traffic Load Testing) or 8.4.3 (Traffic 9 Model Testing) in addition to the traffic applied at the configured RF cell, the same additional traffic should be applied 10 for the ETWS test case. 11  12

 RIC-Enabled End-to-end Use Case Test 1  2 This chapter describes tests for evaluating and assessing the performance of a radio access network with Non-RT RIC 3 and Near-RT RIC optimisations from a network end-to-end perspective. The focus of the testing is either on the end-4 user performance with user specific optimisations by the Non-RT RIC and Near-RT RIC or on system level performance 5 with system specific optimisations.  6 The test setup and test methodology applicable to all the RIC-enabled end-to-end use case tests is described in Section 7 9.1, followed by the various test cases including their test description and procedures for each use case. At the 8 beginning of each use case section, system level, cell level and UE level test configurations and KPIs involved in 9 defining the test scenario and measuring the performance related to the use case testing are discussed. Following it, 10 exact parameter values and KPIs to be measured are defined on a per test case basis.  11 The test applies to SA (Standalone) mode operation only in this version of the test specification. 12 The following RIC-enabled use case tests are defined in this chapter  13 • Traffic steering with connected mode mobility control  14 Future versions of this specification may add additional tests, for example: 15 • Traffic steering with idle mode mobility control 16 • QoS/QoE optimisation 17 • RAN slice SLA assurance 18 • Massive MIMO Beamforming Optimisation 19 Please see below summary table of test cases and applicable technology.  20 Table 9-1: RIC-Enabled End-to-end Use Case Tests summary 21   Applicable technology  Test case Functional group LTE 
NSA 
SA TEST ID RIC-Enabled End-to-end Use Case        9.2 Traffic steering with connected mode mobility control   Traffic Steering Use Case  N/A N/A Y  22  23 9.1 Test Setup and Methodology 24 The overall test setup is shown in Figure 9-1. The SUT in the RIC-enabled end-to-end use case test is comprised of the 25 O-DU(s), O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP, and the Non-RT RIC and Near-RT RIC functions.  The O-RU is not recommended to 26 be part of the SUT, since it does not contribute materially to the outcome of these tests, and several O-RUs would be 27 needed to sufficiently extract the performance benefit of the RIC for each of the use cases.  The test needs to evaluate 28 UE and system performance for mixed types of service, e.g., voice service, video service and data service etc. A 5G 29 core will be required to support the basic functionality to authenticate and register an end user device in order to setup a 30 PDN connection/PDU session. An IMS core will be required to register the end user device to support voice or video 31 services on a 5G network. The 5G and IMS cores may be a completely emulated, partially emulated or a real non-32 emulated core. The application server(s) support for different types of data services is defined in Section 6.1.  33 

  
_______________________________________________________________________________________________.                © 2022 by the O-RAN ALLIANCE e.V. Your use is subject to the copyright statement on the cover page of this specification. 215  
O-RAN.TIFG.E2E-Test.0-v04.00 O-RAN.TIFG.E2E-Test.0-v04.00 
The O-RUs and end user devices (UEs) should be emulated in these tests since the system performance of the various 1 use cases need to be evaluated with a sufficient number of cells and UEs, and under different radio conditions, load 2 conditions and mobility patterns (e.g., at least 10 cells and 100 UEs per cell located at different positions in the network 3 and moving along different trajectories at different speeds) to generate statistics for performance analysis. In addition, 4 with emulated O-RUs and UEs, traffic can be applied directly at the Open Fronthaul interface which is more cost and 5 form-factor efficient due to the large number of cells and UEs that are required. 6 All the elements in the network, including the O-RAN system, 5G core and the application servers need to have the 7 ability to capture traces to validate the successful execution of the test cases. The end user device needs to have the 8 capability to capture traces/packets to calculate the KPIs related to the RIC enabled use cases. 9 
 10 Figure 9-1 Illustration of test setup for RIC enabled use case E2E testing 11 The purpose of these tests are to validate the O-RAN system’s E2E performance for all the various RIC-enabled use 12 cases in different network deployment scenarios [36-38].   In order to achieve this, multiple sets of test configuration 13 parameters are defined for system level, cell level and UE level configuration.  In keeping with the spirit of the end-to-14 end test focus on the O-RAN system as SUT, the detailed ORAN protocol conformance on the various O-RAN internal 15 interfaces a specified in [36-38] will not be verified, and instead, system-level performance according to the objectives 16 of the use case is the primary test focus.      17 There are no passing criteria defined for these performance-focused tests.  This is due to the diversity of vendor-18 specific RRM and MAC layer scheduler implementations in the O-RAN system which also depend on specific network 19 deployment scenarios, making it difficult to specify minimum performance requirements.   20 The performance of the system with Non-RT RIC and Near-RT optimisations should be benchmarked through analysis 21 of KPI measurement results.  The performance gain with Non-RT RIC and Near-RT optimisation is evaluated by 22 comparing the test result with RIC optimisation enabled versus that with it disabled.  2 23 9.2 Traffic Steering Use Case Test 24 The purpose of this test is to validate the O-RAN system’s E2E functionalities and evaluate system performance for the 25 Traffic Steering (TS) use case. In this version of the test specification, only the connected mode 30 mobility control is addressed while idle mode mobility control is FFS.   31

9.2.2.1 Test Description and Applicability 45 The purpose of this test is to validate the O-RAN system’s E2E performance for the Traffic Steering use case in a 46 minimum viable network deployment scenario with two macro cells operating at low-band and mid-band respectively. 47

In the test 3 groups of UEs with different mobility classes (high, low and stationary) and running different services are 1 configured. UEs in the first group run Voice (5QI=1) service and UEs in the second and third UE groups run MBB 2 (5QI=9) service. All UEs stay in RRC connected state. The Non-RT and Near RT RIC are informed of the requirements 3 and characteristics of the services as well as the mobility class of each UE. The RIC entities aim to steer the traffic to 4 the most appropriate cells through Connected Mode mobility control to the guarantee QoS requirements for the service 5 of each UE, maximize service continuity, and meanwhile maximize the overall system throughput for MBB service.  6  7 The test applies to SA (Standalone) mode operation only in this version of the test specification. 8 9.2.2.2 System Level Configuration 9 The following system configuration parameters should be configured and recorded in the test report: 10 Node Configuration (O-RU, O-DU and O-CU-CP/UP) 11 The node configuration of the O-RAN system (O-RU, O-DU and O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP) is not specified. The utilized 12 test configuration (parameters) should be recorded in the test report.   13  14 Traffic Steering Mode 15 Optimization in Near-RT RIC can be done with A1 policy guidance from Non-RT RIC (TS Mode 2) or without the 16 guidance (TS mode 1) [38]. The performance gain with TS Mode 1 or 2 optimization can be compared against TS Mode 17 0 where Non-RT RIC and Near-RT RIC assisted optimisation is disabled. The exact TS Mode configured for the test is 18 not specified. The utilized configuration (parameters) should be recorded in the test report.    19  20 KPI Optimization Policy 21 Guarantee QoS requirement for the service of each UE as defined in [41], Table 5.7.4-1, maximize service continuity,  22 meanwhile maximize the system throughput and service experience for MBB (5QI=9) service.   23 9.1.1.3 Cell Level Configuration 24

9.2.2.5 Test Procedure 1 1. Disable traffic steering optimisation by Near-RT RIC and Non-RT RIC (TS Mode 0 [38]). 2 2. Power on the end user devices and ensure they register with the 5G core for data, video, or voice service over SA 3 by connecting over the O-RAN (O-RU, O-DU and O-CU-CP/O-CU-UP). 4 3. Once the registration is complete, the end user devices establish PDU sessions with the 5G core. 5 4. Once the PDU sessions have been setup, the end user devices with voice or video traffic register with the IMS core 6 to support Voice-over-NR or Video-over-NR service. 7 5. Take appropriate action to enable the traffic according to the traffic configuration profile as discussed in Section 8 9.2.2.4. 9 6. Once the traffic has started, move the devices according to the mobility patterns and mobility classes configured 10 (Section 9.2.2.4). 11 7. Observe the policies and commands from RIC over E2, O1 and A1 interfaces, capture traces over the interfaces (as 12 required) for validating successful test execution. 13 8. Keep the test running for 10 minutes, measure and record the KPIs as defined in Section 9.2.2.7. 14 9. Take appropriate action to reset the network and traffic conditions to its initial state at before Step 3.  15 10. Enable the traffic steering optimisation from Near-RT and Non-RT RIC (TS Mode 1 or TS Mode 2 [38]), repeat 16 Step 4-10. 17 11. Analyse the KPI measurement results. Evaluate the performance gain with Non-RT RIC and Near-RT optimisation 18 enabled versus that with it disabled.   19 9.2.2.6 Expected Result 20 The exact optimization behaviors from Non-RT RIC and Near-RT RIC are not specified due to the diversity of vendor-21 specific RRM and MAC layer scheduler implementations in the O-RAN system making it impossible to specify a 22 standardized behaviour pattern. However, behaviors similar to the example below should be observed from the trace 23 information captured from the emulated UEs.  24 1. For UEs in Group 1, voice traffic (5QI=1) should preferably reside on Cell B to minimize inter-cell handovers 25 for service continuity.  26 2. For UEs in Group 2, the MBB traffic with (5QI=9) should preferably reside on Cell A if the radio and cell load 27 condition on Cell A gives higher throughput and user experience for MBB service than Cell B.     28  29 Serving cell and frequency band change for each UE in UE Group 1 and 2 can be observed from the trace information 30 captured from the emulated UEs in order to confirm the Traffic Steering optimization behaviors from the Non-RT RIC 31 and Near-RT RIC as discussed above.  32  33 The performance of the system with Non-RT RIC and Near-RT optimisation should be benchmarked through analysis 34 of KPI measurement results as discussed in Section 9.2.1.4.  The performance gain with Non-RT RIC and Near-RT 35 optimisation is evaluated by comparing the test result with RIC optimisation enabled versus that with it is disabled. The 36 number of UEs meeting the QoS requirement for voice service (5QI=1) and MBB service (5QI=9), count of service 37 drops, and the total system throughput and service experience for MBB service with Non-RT RIC and Near-RT RIC 38 optimisation in Step 11 (Section 9.2.2.6) should outperform the case without the optimisation in Step 1-8 (Section 39 9.2.2.6).  40 The following statistical KPI measurements should be included in the test report: 41 System level KPIs: 42 • Number of UEs meeting the QoS requirement for voices service (5QI=1) 43 • Number of UEs meeting the QoS requirement for MBB service (5QI=9) 44 • Average system DL PDCP throughput 45 • Average system UL PDCP throughput 46

 1  Cell level KPIs (for cells in performance monitoring group) [35]: 2 • Average DL throughput in gNB per cell 3 • Average UL throughput in gNB per cell  4 • Average DL delay between NG-RAN and UE per cell 5 • Average UL delay between NG-RAN and UE per cell 6 • DL Cell PDCP SDU Data Volume 7 • UL Cell PDCP SDU Data Volume 8  9 End user KPIs (for UEs in performance monitoring group) [35]: 10 • Average DL UE throughput in gNB per UE 11 • Average UL UE throughput in gNB per UE 12 • Average DL delay between NG-RAN and UE per UE 13 • Average UL delay between NG-RAN and UE per UE 14  15 KPIs related to user experience for Voice service:  16 • MOS Score – Mean Opinion Score for the voice call. This needs to be measured on both ends of the Voice call 17 – mobile originated and mobile terminated. 18 • Mute Rate % – This is the percentage of calls which were muted in both directions (calls with RTP loss of > 3-19 4s in both directions are considered muted call). This needs to be measured on both ends of the Voice call – 20 mobile originated and mobile terminated and counted only once per call. 21 • One Way Calls % – This is the percentage of calls which were muted in any one direction (calls with RTP loss 22 of > 3-4s in one direction only are considered one-way calls). This needs to be monitored on both ends of the 23 Voice call – mobile originated and mobile terminated and counted only once per call. 24 • RTP Packet Loss % - Number of RTP packets which were dropped/lost in uplink/downlink direction as a 25 percentage of total packets. This needs to be measured on both ends of the Voice call – mobile originated and 26 mobile terminated. 27  28 KPIs related to user experience for Video service:  29 • Video start time or Time to load first video frame – Time from when the video was selected to play to when 30 the video starts playing in seconds. 31 • Video MOS score – MOS score for the video streaming session as defined by ITU P1203.3. 32  33 KPIs related to user experience for FTP service:  34 • Download throughput – This is the average application layer throughput to download the file in kbps. 35 • Upload throughput – This is the average application layer throughput to upload the file in kbps. 36 • Time taken to Download file – This is the time taken to download the file in seconds. 37 • Time taken to Upload file – This is the time taken to upload the file in seconds. 38  39  40  41  42  43  44  45
